"""Sonic-MoE up-projection: grouped GEMM + fused SwiGLU via Triton.

A single 2-D kernel launch handles every expert simultaneously.
Grid = (E, max_m_tiles * n_tiles).  Each block knows its expert from pid0 and
its (m_tile, n_tile) from pid1; blocks with tile_idx >= expert_num_tiles exit
early.  This avoids Python loops, per-tile metadata buffers, and SM
under-utilisation.
"""
from __future__ import annotations

import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import triton
import triton.language as tl

OP_TYPE = "grouped_gemm_swiglu"
SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["bf16"]
HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000", "H100", "B200"]

# Module-level shape shims (rewritten by check.py / benchmark.py)
T_total = 32768
H = 4096
I = 1536  # noqa: E741
E = 128
K = 8


# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
# Triton kernel  –  2-D grid  (expert, tile_idx)
# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
@triton.jit
def _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel(
    x_ptr,
    w_gate_ptr,
    w_up_ptr,
    expert_offsets_ptr,
    out_ptr,
    stride_x_m,
    stride_x_k,
    stride_wg_e,
    stride_wg_k,
    stride_wg_n,
    stride_wu_e,
    stride_wu_k,
    stride_wu_n,
    stride_o_m,
    stride_o_n,
    H,
    I,
    BLOCK_M: tl.constexpr,
    BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr,
    BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr,
):
    """One block == one (expert, tile_idx).

    pid0 = expert_id    (0 .. E-1)
    pid1 = flat tile index inside that expert
    """
    expert_id = tl.program_id(0)
    tile_idx = tl.program_id(1)

    expert_begin = tl.load(expert_offsets_ptr + expert_id)
    expert_end = tl.load(expert_offsets_ptr + expert_id + 1)
    n_tokens = expert_end - expert_begin
    if n_tokens <= 0:
        return

    num_m_tiles = tl.cdiv(n_tokens, BLOCK_M)
    num_n_tiles = tl.cdiv(I, BLOCK_N)
    total_tiles = num_m_tiles * num_n_tiles
    if tile_idx >= total_tiles:
        return

    m_tile = tile_idx // num_n_tiles
    n_tile = tile_idx % num_n_tiles
    m_start = expert_begin + m_tile * BLOCK_M
    n_start = n_tile * BLOCK_N

    # --- pointer bases -------------------------------------------------------
    x_base = x_ptr + m_start * stride_x_m
    wg_base = w_gate_ptr + expert_id * stride_wg_e + n_start * stride_wg_n
    wu_base = w_up_ptr + expert_id * stride_wu_e + n_start * stride_wu_n
    o_base = out_ptr + m_start * stride_o_m + n_start * stride_o_n

    # --- accumulators --------------------------------------------------------
    acc_gate = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.float32)
    acc_up = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.float32)

    offs_m = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_M)
    offs_n = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)
    offs_k = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K)

    m_mask = (m_start + offs_m)[:, None] < expert_end
    n_mask = (n_start + offs_n)[None, :] < I

    # --- main K-loop ---------------------------------------------------------
    for k in range(0, H, BLOCK_K):
        k_mask = (k + offs_k)[None, :] < H

        # x  : (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K)
        x_ptrs = x_base + offs_m[:, None] * stride_x_m + (k + offs_k)[None, :] * stride_x_k
        x = tl.load(x_ptrs, mask=m_mask & k_mask, other=0.0)

        # wg : (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N)
        wg_ptrs = wg_base + (k + offs_k)[:, None] * stride_wg_k + offs_n[None, :] * stride_wg_n
        wg = tl.load(wg_ptrs, mask=k_mask.T & n_mask, other=0.0)

        # wu : (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N)
        wu_ptrs = wu_base + (k + offs_k)[:, None] * stride_wu_k + offs_n[None, :] * stride_wu_n
        wu = tl.load(wu_ptrs, mask=k_mask.T & n_mask, other=0.0)

        acc_gate = tl.dot(x, wg, acc_gate)
        acc_up = tl.dot(x, wu, acc_up)

    # --- SwiGLU epilogue -----------------------------------------------------
    silu_gate = acc_gate * tl.sigmoid(acc_gate)
    out_val = (silu_gate * acc_up).to(tl.bfloat16)

    # --- store ---------------------------------------------------------------
    o_ptrs = o_base + offs_m[:, None] * stride_o_m + offs_n[None, :] * stride_o_n
    tl.store(o_ptrs, out_val, mask=m_mask & n_mask)


# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
# Model
# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, T_total: int, H: int, I: int, E: int, K: int):  # noqa: E741
        super().__init__()
        self.T_total = T_total
        self.H = H
        self.I = I
        self.E = E
        self.K = K
        self.W_gate = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(E, H, I, dtype=torch.bfloat16))
        self.W_up = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(E, H, I, dtype=torch.bfloat16))
        nn.init.normal_(self.W_gate, std=0.02)
        nn.init.normal_(self.W_up, std=0.02)

        # Cache for grid metadata to avoid CPU sync in forward
        self._cached_offsets: torch.Tensor | None = None
        self._cached_max_n: int | None = None

    def _get_max_n(self, expert_offsets: torch.Tensor) -> int:
        """Return max tokens per expert, cached to avoid repeated D2H syncs."""
        if self._cached_offsets is not None:
            # Fast path: same tensor object (works when inputs are reused)
            if expert_offsets.data_ptr() == self._cached_offsets.data_ptr():
                return self._cached_max_n
            # Slightly slower: same contents but different tensor object
            if expert_offsets.shape == self._cached_offsets.shape:
                if torch.equal(expert_offsets, self._cached_offsets):
                    return self._cached_max_n
        # Cache miss: compute on CPU (one-time cost per unique offsets)
        self._cached_offsets = expert_offsets.clone()
        self._cached_max_n = int((expert_offsets[1:] - expert_offsets[:-1]).max().item())
        return self._cached_max_n

    def forward(
        self,
        hidden_states: torch.Tensor,
        expert_offsets: torch.Tensor,
    ) -> torch.Tensor:
        T_perm, H_val = hidden_states.shape
        I_val = self.I
        E_val = self.E
        out = torch.empty(T_perm, I_val, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=hidden_states.device)

        max_n = self._get_max_n(expert_offsets)

        BLOCK_M = 256
        BLOCK_N = 64
        BLOCK_K = 64
        num_warps = 16
        num_stages = 2

        num_m = (max_n + BLOCK_M - 1) // BLOCK_M
        num_n = (I_val + BLOCK_N - 1) // BLOCK_N
        grid = (E_val, num_m * num_n)

        _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel[grid](
            hidden_states,
            self.W_gate,
            self.W_up,
            expert_offsets,
            out,
            hidden_states.stride(0),
            hidden_states.stride(1),
            self.W_gate.stride(0),
            self.W_gate.stride(1),
            self.W_gate.stride(2),
            self.W_up.stride(0),
            self.W_up.stride(1),
            self.W_up.stride(2),
            out.stride(0),
            out.stride(1),
            H_val,
            I_val,
            BLOCK_M=BLOCK_M,
            BLOCK_N=BLOCK_N,
            BLOCK_K=BLOCK_K,
            num_warps=num_warps,
            num_stages=num_stages,
        )
        return out


# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
# Shape shims
# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
def _build_routing(T_total: int, E: int, K: int, device: str = "cpu") -> torch.Tensor:
    T_perm = T_total * K
    base = T_perm // E
    rem = T_perm - base * E
    counts = torch.full((E,), base, dtype=torch.int32, device=device)
    counts[:rem] += 1
    offsets = torch.zeros(E + 1, dtype=torch.int32, device=device)
    offsets[1:] = torch.cumsum(counts, dim=0)
    return offsets


def get_inputs():
    T_perm = T_total * K
    hidden_states = torch.randn(T_perm, H, dtype=torch.bfloat16) * 0.1
    expert_offsets = _build_routing(T_total, E, K)
    return [hidden_states, expert_offsets]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [T_total, H, I, E, K]

Triton 3.6.0 should support Blackwell. For this task, I think Triton is the most practical path: 1. Fast iteration 2. Good performance on GEMMs 3. No need to install CUTLASS 4. Triton 3.6 supports Blackwell The operation is essentially: for each expert e, compute two GEMMs (gate and up), then fuse with SwiGLU. For grouped GEMM in Triton, there are a few approaches: 1. Use a single launch with a grid that handles all experts, using expert_offsets to determine which rows belong to which expert 2. Use multiple launches (one per expert) - simpler but more overhead 3. Use Triton's grouped GEMM primitives if available Given E=128 experts, launching 128 separate kernels could have significant overhead. A single kernel launch is better. The key insight for a grouped GEMM kernel in Triton: - We can treat the problem as a batched GEMM where each expert has a different M dimension - We can map blocks to output tiles, and for each tile, figure out which expert it belongs to - Or we can use a 1D grid of blocks where each block processes some tiles Let me think about the algorithm: For expert e: - M_e = expert_offsets[e+1] - expert_offsets[e] - K = H - N = I - Compute x_e @ W_gate[e] and x_e @ W_up[e], then silu(gate) * up A good Triton grouped GEMM approach: - Grid: launch enough blocks to cover all output elements across all experts - Each block is assigned to a specific (expert, tile_m, tile_n) combination - The block loads the appropriate x slice and weight slice One common approach is to precompute a "problem sizes" array and use Triton's host-side grouped GEMM launch, but Triton doesn't have a native grouped GEMM API like CUTLASS. Instead, I can: 1. Flatten all experts' output tiles into a 1D grid 2. Each block determines which expert and tile it belongs to 3. Process that tile Let me look at some reference implementations for grouped GEMM in Triton. The `fused_moe` implementations from DeepSeek/vLLM are good references. Let me check the CUTLASS paths more carefully and see what's available. Also, let me look at the deep_gemm and flashinfer references for grouped GEMM approaches. Actually, let me think about this more carefully. The fastest path to a working, high-performance solution is probably Triton. Let me investigate Triton grouped GEMM patterns. For a Triton grouped GEMM with SwiGLU fusion, here's the approach: 1. We have E experts, each with M_e tokens 2. Total tokens T_perm = sum(M_e) 3. For each expert e, compute: - gate = x[e] @ W_gate[e] (M_e, I) - up = x[e] @ W_up[e] (M_e, I) - out = silu(gate) * up (M_e, I) The kernel design: - Use a 1D grid where each block handles one (expert, tile_m, tile_n) - Precompute `tile_offsets` on CPU: for each expert, how many tiles it has - Each block finds its expert by binary search or by computing from block_idx - Actually, a simpler approach: use a 2D grid where dim0 = experts and dim1 = tiles_per_expert - But experts have different M_e, so some experts need more tiles than others Better approach: - Launch a 1D grid with total_num_tiles blocks - Each block is assigned to a specific (expert, tile_m, tile_n) - We can precompute a mapping from block_id -> (expert_id, tile_m, tile_n) Wait, actually there's an even simpler approach that works well: - Flatten the problem: each expert e has M_e rows and I columns - Total output elements = T_perm * I - We can launch a grid of blocks where each block handles a tile of (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N) output elements - The block needs to figure out which expert and which rows/columns it processes Let me think about how to map output coordinates to experts: - Expert e covers output rows [expert_offsets[e], expert_offsets[e+1]) - So for a given output row r, we need to find which expert it belongs to - We can do this with a binary search on expert_offsets In Triton, binary search is possible but expensive. Better approach: - Since the distribution is balanced (base = T_perm // E, with remainder distributed to first experts), we could potentially compute it directly, but that's fragile. Another approach: precompute a `sorted_expert_ids` array of length T_perm where sorted_expert_ids[i] = expert id of row i. But that takes O(T_perm) memory and the lookup is just an array access. Actually, for the balanced case (which is what check.py uses), we could compute the expert id directly. But to be robust, let me think of a general approach. A very common approach in Triton grouped GEMM is to use a "tile_to_problem" mapping: 1. On CPU, compute for each tile: which problem (expert) it belongs to, and which tile index within that problem 2. Pass this as a tensor to the kernel 3. Each block reads its assignment But this requires extra CPU work and memory. For the balanced routing used in check.py, we can actually compute expert_id from row index very simply: - base = T_perm // E - rem = T_perm - base * E - Expert e covers rows [offsets[e], offsets[e+1]) - offsets[e] = e * base + min(e, rem) So for row r, expert_id is the largest e such that offsets[e] <= r. This can be computed as: if r < rem * (base + 1), then e = r // (base + 1), else e = rem + (r - rem * (base + 1)) // base But check.py might not always use the _build_routing function... actually it does. Let me re-read check.py. Yes, check.py uses `reference.get_inputs()` which calls `_build_routing`. So the routing is always balanced with this specific pattern. However, the Model.forward should probably be general enough to handle any expert_offsets. Let me make the kernel robust. For a Triton kernel, I can: 1. Pass expert_offsets to the kernel 2. Each block does a binary search in expert_offsets to find which expert it belongs to 3. Or pass a precomputed `expert_ids` array Actually, the simplest robust approach: precompute an `expert_ids` tensor of shape (T_perm,) on the CPU in the model's forward, before launching the kernel. For T_perm = 32768 * 8 = 262144, this is tiny. Wait, but the Model.forward gets expert_offsets, not expert_ids. I can compute expert_ids from expert_offsets. Actually, let me think about this differently. Instead of mapping rows to experts, I can launch a kernel with grid = (num_tiles_total,) and each block processes a (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N) tile of output. The block needs to: 1. Determine its output row range [start_row, end_row) 2. Determine which expert those rows belong to 3. Load the corresponding x and weights 4. Compute gate and up GEMMs 5. Apply SwiGLU and store For step 2, if I precompute expert_ids, it's just an array lookup at start_row. I need all rows in a tile to belong to the same expert, which means BLOCK_M must divide the expert's token count, or I need to handle tile boundaries carefully. Actually, a cleaner approach is to not precompute expert_ids but instead use a "tile metadata" array: - For each output tile, store (expert_id, m_start, n_start) - The kernel reads this metadata To generate tile metadata on CPU: - For each expert e: - M_e = offsets[e+1] - offsets[e] - num_m_tiles = ceil(M_e / BLOCK_M) - num_n_tiles = ceil(I / BLOCK_N) - For each m_tile in [0, num_m_tiles): - For each n_tile in [0, num_n_tiles): - Store (e, m_tile * BLOCK_M, n_tile * BLOCK_N) The kernel then: 1. Reads its tile metadata 2. Loads x[expert_id][m_start:m_start+BLOCK_M] (shape BLOCK_M x H) 3. Loads W_gate[expert_id][:, n_start:n_start+BLOCK_N] (shape H x BLOCK_N) 4. Loads W_up[expert_id][:, n_start:n_start+BLOCK_N] (shape H x BLOCK_N) 5. Computes gate = x @ W_gate, up = x @ W_up 6. Applies silu(gate) * up 7. Stores result This is very clean and general. The overhead of generating tile metadata on CPU is negligible. Let me also think about performance: - For the headline shape: T_perm = 262144, H = 4096, I = 1536, E = 128 - Average M per expert = 262144 / 128 = 2048 - So each expert does GEMM(2048, 1536, 4096) - With BLOCK_M=64, BLOCK_N=64, each expert has 32*24 = 768 tiles - Total tiles = 128 * 768 = 98304 - On RTX PRO 6000, we have many SMs, so this should parallelize well For performance, we want: - BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K tuned - Use tensor cores (wmma or mma) via tl.dot - Coalesced memory access - Good occupancy Triton's tl.dot automatically uses tensor cores on SM120. For bf16, it should use the BF16 tensor core instructions. Now, the challenge is that we need to compute TWO GEMMs (gate and up) per tile. We can either: 1. Load x once, then load W_gate and W_up, compute gate and up simultaneously (fused in the same kernel) 2. Or load x once, compute gate, then reload x and compute up Option 1 is better: load x once into shared memory, then loop over K dimension once, accumulating both gate and up simultaneously. Wait, W_gate and W_up have the same shape (H, I). For a given output tile (m_start, n_start), both GEMMs use the same x slice but different weight slices. So: 1. Load x_tile into shared memory (or keep in registers) 2. For each K block: - Load W_gate_block and W_up_block - Accumulate gate_acc += x_block @ W_gate_block - Accumulate up_acc += x_block @ W_up_block 3. After K loop, apply silu(gate_acc) * up_acc 4. Store output This is very efficient! We load x only once and reuse it for both GEMMs. Let me think about memory layout: - x is (T_perm, H) row-major - W_gate and W_up are (E, H, I) - so for expert e, W[e] is (H, I) row-major - Output is (T_perm, I) row-major For coalesced access: - Loading x: contiguous across H dimension (row-major), so load x[m_start:m_start+BLOCK_M, k_start:k_start+BLOCK_K] - contiguous within rows - Loading W: W[e, k_start:k_start+BLOCK_K, n_start:n_start+BLOCK_N] - contiguous within the N dimension for fixed k... actually W is (H, I) row-major, so W[k, n_start:n_start+BLOCK_N] is contiguous. Good. For tl.dot, we need the inner dimensions to match. The standard pattern is: - x_tile: (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K) - w_tile: (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N) - acc: (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N) This works perfectly. Let me now design the Triton kernel: ```python import triton import triton.language as tl @triton.jit def grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel( hidden_states_ptr, # (T_perm, H) W_gate_ptr, # (E, H, I) W_up_ptr, # (E, H, I) expert_offsets_ptr, # (E+1,) output_ptr, # (T_perm, I) # strides stride_x_m, stride_x_k, stride_wg_e, stride_wg_k, stride_wg_n, stride_wu_e, stride_wu_k, stride_wu_n, stride_o_m, stride_o_n, # dims H, I, # tile metadata tile_expert_ptr, # (num_tiles,) tile_m_start_ptr, # (num_tiles,) tile_n_start_ptr, # (num_tiles,) # block sizes BLOCK_M: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) expert_id = tl.load(tile_expert_ptr + pid) m_start = tl.load(tile_m_start_ptr + pid) n_start = tl.load(tile_n_start_ptr + pid) # Compute offsets x_base = hidden_states_ptr + m_start * stride_x_m wg_base = W_gate_ptr + expert_id * stride_wg_e + n_start * stride_wg_n wu_base = W_up_ptr + expert_id * stride_wu_e + n_start * stride_wu_n o_base = output_ptr + m_start * stride_o_m + n_start * stride_o_n # Accumulators acc_gate = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.float32) acc_up = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.float32) # Loop over K for k in range(0, H, BLOCK_K): # Load x tile offs_m = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_M) + m_start offs_k = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K) + k x_ptrs = x_base + offs_m[:, None] * stride_x_m + offs_k[None, :] * stride_x_k x_mask = (offs_m[:, None] < ...) & (offs_k[None, :] < ...) x = tl.load(x_ptrs, mask=x_mask, other=0.0) # Load W_gate tile offs_n = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) + n_start wg_ptrs = wg_base + offs_k[:, None] * stride_wg_k + offs_n[None, :] * stride_wg_n wg_mask = (offs_k[:, None] < ...) & (offs_n[None, :] < ...) wg = tl.load(wg_ptrs, mask=wg_mask, other=0.0) # Load W_up tile wu_ptrs = wu_base + offs_k[:, None] * stride_wu_k + offs_n[None, :] * stride_wu_n wu = tl.load(wu_ptrs, mask=wg_mask, other=0.0) # same mask # Compute dot products acc_gate += tl.dot(x, wg) acc_up += tl.dot(x, wu) # Apply SwiGLU # silu(x) = x * sigmoid(x) gate_result = acc_gate * tl.sigmoid(acc_gate) out = gate_result * acc_up # Store output offs_m = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_M) + m_start offs_n = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) + n_start o_ptrs = o_base + offs_m[:, None] * stride_o_m + offs_n[None, :] * stride_o_n o_mask = (offs_m[:, None] < ...) & (offs_n[None, :] < ...) tl.store(o_ptrs, out.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=o_mask) ``` Wait, I need to be careful about the masks. Since each expert has a different M_e, and tiles at the boundary might exceed the expert's row count, I need to mask properly. Also, I need to compute the end row for each expert: `expert_end = expert_offsets[expert_id + 1]`. So the mask for rows should be `offs_m < expert_end`. Actually, since tiles don't cross expert boundaries (we generate tile metadata per expert), the row mask is just `offs_m < expert_end` and `offs_n < I`. For generating tile metadata, I need: ```python def generate_tile_metadata(expert_offsets, I, BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N): tiles = [] E = len(expert_offsets) - 1 for e in range(E): start = int(expert_offsets[e].item()) end = int(expert_offsets[e + 1].item()) M_e = end - start if M_e == 0: continue num_m_tiles = (M_e + BLOCK_M - 1) // BLOCK_M num_n_tiles = (I + BLOCK_N - 1) // BLOCK_N for m_tile in range(num_m_tiles): m_start = start + m_tile * BLOCK_M for n_tile in range(num_n_tiles): n_start = n_tile * BLOCK_N tiles.append((e, m_start, n_start)) return torch.tensor(tiles, dtype=torch.int32) ``` Wait, but this generates a lot of tiles and loops in Python. For 128 experts with ~768 tiles each, that's ~100k iterations, which is fine. Actually, I can optimize this with vectorized numpy/torch operations, but a simple Python loop is fine since it's done once per forward pass and the numbers are small. The expert_offsets might change between forward passes (different token distributions), but in practice for inference, they might be similar. For training, they change every step. For the benchmark, it's timed with warm-up, so the overhead might be amortized. But ideally, I want to minimize CPU work. Alternative: don't use tile metadata.      2	
     3	Concatenated gate+up weights: single matmul per expert, split in epilogue.

     7	Fewer kernel launches (128 vs 256), simpler kernel code (one tl.dot).

reasoning (17607 chars)
Let me analyze the two previous solutions: 1. **DeepSeek-V4-Pro solution**: Loops over experts in Python, launching one Triton kernel per expert. Each kernel uses autotune. The weights are concatenated (gate | up) so each expert launch does one kernel with two `tl.dot` calls inside. Simple but has Python loop overhead for 128 experts. 2. **DeepSeek-V4-Flash solution**: Launches a single kernel with grid=(E,), where each block handles one expert. Inside the kernel, it loops over M and N tiles. It also tiles the weights by permuting them to (E, num_nt, H, bn) for better memory layout. This avoids Python loop overhead but uses a single block per expert, which might underutilize the GPU for small experts. Both approaches have trade-offs. Let me think about what's best. For the headline shape (E=128, M_e≈2048): - Solution 1 launches 128 kernels, each with ~192 blocks (for 128x128 tiles). Total ~24k blocks. Good parallelism. - Solution 2 launches 128 blocks total, each doing a lot of work serially. Underutilized. For the smaller shape (E=64, M_e=256): - Solution 1 launches 64 kernels, each with ~16 blocks. Total ~1k blocks. - Solution 2 launches 64 blocks total. Very underutilized. So Solution 1's approach seems better for parallelism. The main downside is Python loop overhead, but with 128 iterations and kernel launch overhead, it might still be okay. However, we can do better: launch a SINGLE kernel that handles all experts' tiles. This avoids both Python loop overhead and underutilization. My approach: 1. Precompute tile metadata on CPU: for each output tile, store (expert_id, m_start, n_start) 2. Launch one kernel with grid=(num_tiles,) 3. Each block reads its tile assignment and processes it 4. Fused SwiGLU in the epilogue This is similar to CUTLASS grouped GEMM's approach. Let me think about the weight layout. The weights are (E, H, I) row-major. For contiguous access when loading (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N) tiles, we want: - W[k_start:k_start+BLOCK_K, n_start:n_start+BLOCK_N] to be contiguous or at least coalesced - In row-major (H, I), W[k, n_start:n_start+BLOCK_N] is contiguous for each k - So loading W with shape (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N) where the N dimension is contiguous is good Actually, there's an optimization: transpose the weights to (E, I, H) so that when we load (BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K) for tl.dot, the K dimension is contiguous. Wait, no - for `tl.dot(x, w)` where x is (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K) and w is (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N): - x needs K to be contiguous or have small stride - w needs K to be the leading dimension (column-major for the dot) Actually, Triton's `tl.dot` expects: - x: (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K) - for standard matmul, the K dimension should be contiguous or have stride 1 within the warp - w: (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N) - the K dimension should be contiguous Wait, let me be more precise. `tl.dot(a, b)` where a has shape (M, K) and b has shape (K, N): - In Triton, a is typically loaded with `a = tl.load(a_ptrs)` where a_ptrs has shape (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K) - b is loaded with `b = tl.load(b_ptrs)` where b_ptrs has shape (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N) - The K dimension of a and the K dimension of b are the contraction dimension - For tensor cores, Triton handles the layout internally In practice, for row-major matrices: - x (M, K) row-major: x_ptrs = x_base + offs_m[:, None] * stride_m + offs_k[None, :] * stride_k - If stride_k = 1, then within each row, elements are contiguous. This is good. - w (K, N) row-major: w_ptrs = w_base + offs_k[:, None] * stride_k + offs_n[None, :] * stride_n - If stride_n = 1, then within each row, elements are contiguous. - But for tl.dot, w should have shape (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N), meaning K is the leading dimension. - With row-major (K, N), stride_k = N, stride_n = 1. - w_ptrs = w_base + offs_k[:, None] * N + offs_n[None, :] * 1 - This means each column is contiguous (stride 1 in n), but columns are N apart in k. - This is actually fine for Triton! Wait, but for tensor core efficiency, sometimes transposing the weight matrix helps. Let me not overthink this - Triton's tl.dot should handle the standard row-major layout fine. Actually, one optimization that is commonly used: store weights in a "tiled" or "swizzled" layout for better shared memory bank conflicts. But that's more advanced. Let me start with the simple approach. Now, about the single-kernel grouped GEMM approach: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import triton import triton.language as tl @triton.jit def grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel( x_ptr, w_gate_ptr, w_up_ptr, expert_offsets_ptr, out_ptr, stride_x_m, stride_x_k, stride_wg_e, stride_wg_k, stride_wg_n, stride_wu_e, stride_wu_k, stride_wu_n, stride_o_m, stride_o_n, H, I, tile_expert_ptr, tile_m_start_ptr, tile_n_start_ptr, BLOCK_M: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) expert_id = tl.load(tile_expert_ptr + pid) m_start = tl.load(tile_m_start_ptr + pid) n_start = tl.load(tile_n_start_ptr + pid) expert_start = tl.load(expert_offsets_ptr + expert_id) expert_end = tl.load(expert_offsets_ptr + expert_id + 1) offs_m = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_M) + m_start offs_n = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) + n_start offs_k = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K) x_ptrs = x_ptr + offs_m[:, None] * stride_x_m + offs_k[None, :] * stride_x_k wg_ptrs = w_gate_ptr + expert_id * stride_wg_e + offs_k[:, None] * stride_wg_k + offs_n[None, :] * stride_wg_n wu_ptrs = w_up_ptr + expert_id * stride_wu_e + offs_k[:, None] * stride_wu_k + offs_n[None, :] * stride_wu_n o_ptrs = out_ptr + offs_m[:, None] * stride_o_m + offs_n[None, :] * stride_o_n acc_gate = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.float32) acc_up = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.float32) for k in range(0, H, BLOCK_K): k_mask = offs_k[None, :] + k < H m_mask = offs_m[:, None] < expert_end n_mask = offs_n[None, :] < I x = tl.load(x_ptrs + k * stride_x_k, mask=m_mask & k_mask, other=0.0) wg = tl.load(wg_ptrs + k * stride_wg_k, mask=k_mask.T & n_mask, other=0.0) wu = tl.load(wu_ptrs + k * stride_wu_k, mask=k_mask.T & n_mask, other=0.0) acc_gate = tl.dot(x, wg, acc_gate) acc_up = tl.dot(x, wu, acc_up) silu_gate = acc_gate * tl.sigmoid(acc_gate) out = (silu_gate * acc_up).to(tl.bfloat16) tl.store(o_ptrs, out, mask=m_mask & n_mask) ``` Wait, I need to be careful with masks in the dot product. Triton's `tl.dot` with masks might have issues. Let me think about this more carefully. In Triton, `tl.dot` typically doesn't take a mask directly (unlike load/store). The masking is done by loading zeros for out-of-bounds elements. So the pattern is: 1. Load with mask, using `other=0.0` 2. Call `tl.dot` on the loaded values This is what I wrote above. Good. But wait - `offs_k[None, :] + k < H` creates a mask of shape (1, BLOCK_K). And `m_mask` is (BLOCK_M, 1). Their `&` gives (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K). Similarly for weights. Actually, let me be more careful about shapes: - `x_ptrs` shape: (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K), computed as `x_ptr + offs_m[:, None] * stride_x_m + offs_k[None, :] * stride_x_k` - `wg_ptrs` shape: (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N), computed as `w_gate_ptr + expert_id * stride_wg_e + offs_k[:, None] * stride_wg_k + offs_n[None, :] * stride_wg_n` For `tl.dot(x, wg)`: - x must be (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K) - wg must be (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N) - The K dimensions must match In my code: - x: (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K) - good - wg: (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N) - good But for the mask on wg: - `k_mask.T` would transpose (1, BLOCK_K) to (BLOCK_K, 1)... wait, `offs_k[None, :] + k < H` is shape (1, BLOCK_K). `.T` gives (BLOCK_K, 1). - `n_mask` is (1, BLOCK_N). - `k_mask.T & n_mask` is (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N). Good. Actually, in Triton, we don't need `.T` - we can just write: `k_mask = offs_k[:, None] + k < H` # shape (BLOCK_K, 1) `n_mask = offs_n[None, :] < I` # shape (1, BLOCK_N) `wg_mask = k_mask & n_mask` # shape (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N) That's cleaner. Let me also think about `stride_wg_k`. The weights are (E, H, I) in PyTorch. So: - stride_wg_e = H * I - stride_wg_k = I - stride_wg_n = 1 For `wg_ptrs = w_gate_ptr + expert_id * H * I + offs_k[:, None] * I + offs_n[None, :] * 1` This points to `W_gate[expert_id, offs_k, offs_n]`. Good. Now, `wg_ptrs + k * stride_wg_k` advances by k along the H (k) dimension. Since stride_wg_k = I, this advances by k*I, which is correct for moving to `W_gate[expert_id, offs_k + k, offs_n]`. For x: - stride_x_m = H (row-major) - stride_x_k = 1 - x_ptrs = x_ptr + offs_m[:, None] * H + offs_k[None, :] * 1 - x_ptrs + k * stride_x_k = x_ptrs + k * 1 = x_ptr + offs_m[:, None] * H + (offs_k + k)[None, :] * 1 This points to `x[offs_m, offs_k + k]`. Good. For output: - stride_o_m = I - stride_o_n = 1 - o_ptrs = out_ptr + offs_m[:, None] * I + offs_n[None, :] * 1 This points to `out[offs_m, offs_n]`. Good. One potential issue: the tile metadata arrays (`tile_expert_ptr`, etc.) need to be on the GPU. I'll create them on the CPU and move to GPU. Let me think about autotuning. For the grouped GEMM with tile metadata, the autotune key could be based on H and I, since those determine the tile geometry. ```python @triton.autotune( configs=[ triton.Config({"BLOCK_M": 64, "BLOCK_N": 64, "BLOCK_K": 64}, num_stages=3, num_warps=4), triton.Config({"BLOCK_M": 64, "BLOCK_N": 128, "BLOCK_K": 64}, num_stages=3, num_warps=4), triton.Config({"BLOCK_M": 128, "BLOCK_N": 64, "BLOCK_K": 64}, num_stages=3, num_warps=4), triton.Config({"BLOCK_M": 128, "BLOCK_N": 128, "BLOCK_K": 64}, num_stages=3, num_warps=4), triton.Config({"BLOCK_M": 128, "BLOCK_N": 128, "BLOCK_K": 128}, num_stages=3, num_warps=4), triton.Config({"BLOCK_M": 256, "BLOCK_N": 64, "BLOCK_K": 64}, num_stages=3, num_warps=8), triton.Config({"BLOCK_M": 64, "BLOCK_N": 256, "BLOCK_K": 64}, num_stages=3, num_warps=8), triton.Config({"BLOCK_M": 128, "BLOCK_N": 256, "BLOCK_K": 64}, num_stages=3, num_warps=8), ], key=["H", "I"], ) ``` Actually, I should think about what block sizes work best on Blackwell. SM120 has: - 128 KB shared memory per SM (I think, need to verify) - Larger L2 cache - Newer tensor cores For bf16 tensor cores, typical block sizes are: - BLOCK_M = 64, 128, 256 - BLOCK_N = 64, 128, 256 - BLOCK_K = 64, 128 Shared memory needed: - x tile: BLOCK_M * BLOCK_K * 2 bytes (bf16) - wg tile: BLOCK_K * BLOCK_N * 2 bytes - wu tile: BLOCK_K * BLOCK_N * 2 bytes - Total: 2 * (BLOCK_M * BLOCK_K + 2 * BLOCK_K * BLOCK_N) bytes For BLOCK_M=128, BLOCK_N=128, BLOCK_K=64: - x: 128 * 64 * 2 = 16 KB - wg: 64 * 128 * 2 = 16 KB - wu: 64 * 128 * 2 = 16 KB - Total: 48 KB For BLOCK_M=256, BLOCK_N=128, BLOCK_K=64: - x: 256 * 64 * 2 = 32 KB - wg: 64 * 128 * 2 = 16 KB - wu: 64 * 128 * 2 = 16 KB - Total: 64 KB For BLOCK_M=128, BLOCK_N=256, BLOCK_K=64: - x: 128 * 64 * 2 = 16 KB - wg: 64 * 256 * 2 = 32 KB - wu: 64 * 256 * 2 = 32 KB - Total: 80 KB All well within 128 KB. But we also need registers for accumulators. With 2 accumulators of fp32: - acc_gate: BLOCK_M * BLOCK_N * 4 bytes - acc_up: BLOCK_M * BLOCK_N * 4 bytes For 128x128: 128 * 128 * 4 * 2 = 128 KB of register pressure... wait, that's shared across all threads in a block. With 4 warps (128 threads), each thread holds 128*128*4*2 / 128 = 1024 bytes = 256 fp32 values. That's 256 registers per thread for accumulators alone. Plus other registers. This might be too much. Actually, Triton manages register allocation automatically. The compiler might spill to shared memory if there are too many registers. Let me be more conservative. For num_warps=4 (128 threads): - BLOCK_M=64, BLOCK_N=64: 64*64*4*2/128 = 256 bytes/thread = 64 regs. Fine. - BLOCK_M=128, BLOCK_N=128: 128*128*4*2/128 = 1024 bytes/thread = 256 regs. This is a lot but might be okay on Blackwell. Let me not overthink this. I'll include a variety of configs and let autotune pick. Actually, wait. I realize there might be an issue with my kernel design. When we have multiple experts with very different M_e sizes, the tile mapping needs to be generated every forward pass. This could add CPU overhead. For the benchmark, `time_fn` does warm-up iterations, so the autotune will happen during warm-up and the actual timed runs will use the best config. But the tile metadata generation happens every call. To optimize: 1. Cache tile metadata if expert_offsets hasn't changed 2. Or, compute tile metadata on GPU For caching, I can store the previous expert_offsets and reuse the tile metadata if they're the same. In practice, for inference with fixed batch size, expert_offsets is often the same or similar. For now, let me keep it simple and generate on CPU. The generation is O(num_tiles) which is at most ~100k, very fast. Let me also think about an alternative: instead of tile metadata, I can compute the expert and tile from the block ID using a prefix sum array. Actually, let me look at how CUTLASS grouped GEMM does it. They use a "problem sizes" array and each block computes which problem it belongs to. But they have sophisticated load balancing. For my purposes, tile metadata is simpler and correct. Let me go with that. Now let me think about one more optimization: weight pre-transposition. If I transpose W_gate and W_up from (E, H, I) to (E, I, H), then when loading the weight tile for `tl.dot`, the K dimension (now H) is contiguous. Wait, no - for `tl.dot(x, w)` where x is (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K) and w is (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N): - x loads from (M, K) with K contiguous - w loads from (K, N) with N contiguous If W is (E, H, I) row-major: - W[e, k, n] has stride H*I in e, I in k, 1 in n - For w tile (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N): w[e, k:k+BLOCK_K, n:n+BLOCK_N] - The n dimension is contiguous (stride 1), k dimension has stride I - This is row-major within the tile, which is fine for Triton If I transpose to (E, I, H): - W_t[e, n, k] has stride I*H in e, H in n, 1 in k - For w tile (BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K): w_t[e, n:n+BLOCK_N, k:k+BLOCK_K] - The k dimension is contiguous (stride 1), n dimension has stride H - But tl.dot expects w to be (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N), not (BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K) If I use `tl.dot(x, w.T)` where w is loaded as (BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K), that would work. But transposing in shared memory might have overhead. Actually, for tensor cores, Triton handles the layout internally. The standard row-major (H, I) layout should work fine with `tl.dot`. Let me also consider: what if I use a single weight tensor of shape (E, H, 2*I) like the first solution? Then I do one tl.dot for gate and one for up, but they share the same x load. This reduces the number of weight loads from memory. Wait, both solutions already load x once and reuse it for both dots. The difference is: - Two separate weight tensors: need 2 loads per K block - Concatenated weight tensor: still need 2 loads per K block (gate slice and up slice) There's no difference in memory bandwidth for weights. But concatenated might have slightly better cache behavior since gate and up weights for the same (k, n) are adjacent in memory. Actually, for the concatenated approach: - W_combined[e, k, n] where n in [0, I) is gate, n in [I, 2*I) is up - W_gate_slice = W_combined[e, k, n_start:n_start+BLOCK_N] - W_up_slice = W_combined[e, k, I+n_start:I+n_start+BLOCK_N] - These are BLOCK_N apart in memory, not adjacent So the cache benefit is minimal. I'll stick with separate weights for simplicity. Now let me write the solution. I'll create a single-kernel grouped GEMM with tile metadata. Wait, I just realized something. The DeepSeek-V4-Pro solution loops over experts in Python but uses autotune per expert. The autotune will add compilation overhead for each expert shape. But since all experts have the same shape (same M_e for balanced routing), the cache key is the same, so it only compiles once. Actually, looking more carefully at the autotune key: `key=["M", "N", "K"]`. For each expert, M = n_e which might vary slightly due to remainder distribution. So experts with different n_e might trigger different compilations. For balanced routing, most experts have the same n_e, but the first `rem` experts have n_e+1. So there could be 2 different compiled kernels. Still fine. But the Python loop overhead of 128 iterations might be non-trivial, especially for small shapes. +"""Sonic-MoE up-projection: grouped GEMM + fused SwiGLU via Triton.
+
+Single kernel launch with tile metadata.  Each block is assigned to one
+(expert, m-tile, n-tile) via precomputed lookup tables, so there is no
+Python loop over experts and no SM under-utilisation for small experts.
+"""
+from __future__ import annotations
+
+import torch
+import torch.nn as nn
+import triton
+import triton.language as tl
+
+OP_TYPE = "grouped_gemm_swiglu"
+SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["bf16"]
+HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000", "H100", "B200"]
+
+# Module-level shape shims (rewritten by check.py / benchmark.py)
+T_total = 32768
+H = 4096
+I = 1536  # noqa: E741
+E = 128
+K = 8
+
+
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+# Triton kernel
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+@triton.autotune(
+    configs=[
+        triton.Config({"BLOCK_M": 64,  "BLOCK_N": 64,  "BLOCK_K": 64}, num_stages=3, num_warps=4),
+        triton.Config({"BLOCK_M": 64,  "BLOCK_N": 128, "BLOCK_K": 64}, num_stages=3, num_warps=4),
+        triton.Config({"BLOCK_M": 128, "BLOCK_N": 64,  "BLOCK_K": 64}, num_stages=3, num_warps=4),
+        triton.Config({"BLOCK_M": 128, "BLOCK_N": 128, "BLOCK_K": 64}, num_stages=3, num_warps=4),
+        triton.Config({"BLOCK_M": 128, "BLOCK_N": 128, "BLOCK_K": 128}, num_stages=3, num_warps=4),
+        triton.Config({"BLOCK_M": 256, "BLOCK_N": 64,  "BLOCK_K": 64}, num_stages=3, num_warps=8),
+        triton.Config({"BLOCK_M": 64,  "BLOCK_N": 256, "BLOCK_K": 64}, num_stages=3, num_warps=8),
+        triton.Config({"BLOCK_M": 128, "BLOCK_N": 256, "BLOCK_K": 64}, num_stages=3, num_warps=8),
+        triton.Config({"BLOCK_M": 256, "BLOCK_N": 128, "BLOCK_K": 64}, num_stages=3, num_warps=8),
+        triton.Config({"BLOCK_M": 128, "BLOCK_N": 128, "BLOCK_K": 64}, num_stages=4, num_warps=4),
+        triton.Config({"BLOCK_M": 64,  "BLOCK_N": 128, "BLOCK_K": 64}, num_stages=4, num_warps=4),
+        triton.Config({"BLOCK_M": 128, "BLOCK_N": 64,  "BLOCK_K": 64}, num_stages=4, num_warps=4),
+    ],
+    key=["H", "I"],
+)
+@triton.jit
+def _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel(
+    x_ptr,
+    w_gate_ptr,
+    w_up_ptr,
+    expert_offsets_ptr,
+    out_ptr,
+    stride_x_m,
+    stride_x_k,
+    stride_wg_e,
+    stride_wg_k,
+    stride_wg_n,
+    stride_wu_e,
+    stride_wu_k,
+    stride_wu_n,
+    stride_o_m,
+    stride_o_n,
+    H,
+    I,
+    tile_expert_ptr,
+    tile_m_start_ptr,
+    tile_n_start_ptr,
+    BLOCK_M: tl.constexpr,
+    BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr,
+    BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr,
+):
+    """One block  ==  one (expert, m-tile, n-tile).
+
+    Loads the x-slice belonging to the expert, accumulates gate and up
+    simultaneously, applies silu(gate)*up, and stores the result.
+    """
+    pid = tl.program_id(0)
+
+    # --- read tile assignment ------------------------------------------------
+    expert_id = tl.load(tile_expert_ptr + pid)
+    m_start = tl.load(tile_m_start_ptr + pid)
+    n_start = tl.load(tile_n_start_ptr + pid)
+
+    expert_begin = tl.load(expert_offsets_ptr + expert_id)
+    expert_end = tl.load(expert_offsets_ptr + expert_id + 1)
+
+    # --- pointer bases -------------------------------------------------------
+    x_base = x_ptr + m_start * stride_x_m
+    wg_base = w_gate_ptr + expert_id * stride_wg_e + n_start * stride_wg_n
+    wu_base = w_up_ptr + expert_id * stride_wu_e + n_start * stride_wu_n
+    o_base = out_ptr + m_start * stride_o_m + n_start * stride_o_n
+
+    # --- accumulators --------------------------------------------------------
+    acc_gate = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.float32)
+    acc_up = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.float32)
+
+    offs_m = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_M)
+    offs_n = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)
+    offs_k = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K)
+
+    m_mask = (m_start + offs_m)[:, None] < expert_end
+    n_mask = (n_start + offs_n)[None, :] < I
+
+    # --- main K-loop ---------------------------------------------------------
+    for k in range(0, H, BLOCK_K):
+        k_mask = (k + offs_k)[None, :] < H
+
+        # x  : (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K)
+        x_ptrs = x_base + offs_m[:, None] * stride_x_m + (k + offs_k)[None, :] * stride_x_k
+        x = tl.load(x_ptrs, mask=m_mask & k_mask, other=0.0)
+
+        # wg : (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N)
+        wg_ptrs = wg_base + (k + offs_k)[:, None] * stride_wg_k + offs_n[None, :] * stride_wg_n
+        wg = tl.load(wg_ptrs, mask=k_mask.T & n_mask, other=0.0)
+
+        # wu : (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N)
+        wu_ptrs = wu_base + (k + offs_k)[:, None] * stride_wu_k + offs_n[None, :] * stride_wu_n
+        wu = tl.load(wu_ptrs, mask=k_mask.T & n_mask, other=0.0)
+
+        acc_gate = tl.dot(x, wg, acc_gate)
+        acc_up = tl.dot(x, wu, acc_up)
+
+    # --- SwiGLU epilogue -----------------------------------------------------
+    silu_gate = acc_gate * tl.sigmoid(acc_gate)
+    out_val = (silu_gate * acc_up).to(tl.bfloat16)
+
+    # --- store ---------------------------------------------------------------
+    o_ptrs = o_base + offs_m[:, None] * stride_o_m + offs_n[None, :] * stride_o_n
+    tl.store(o_ptrs, out_val, mask=m_mask & n_mask)
+
+
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+# Host helpers
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+def _generate_tile_metadata(expert_offsets: torch.Tensor, I: int, BLOCK_M: int, BLOCK_N: int):
+    """Return (tile_expert, tile_m_start, tile_n_start) on the same device as offsets."""
+    E = expert_offsets.numel() - 1
+    device = expert_offsets.device
+    # Build lists on CPU then move – negligible cost for <100 k tiles.
+    experts, m_starts, n_starts = [], [], []
+    off_cpu = expert_offsets.cpu()
+    for e in range(E):
+        begin = int(off_cpu[e].item())
+        end = int(off_cpu[e + 1].item())
+        if end <= begin:
+            continue
+        num_m = (end

 """Sonic-MoE up-projection: grouped GEMM + fused SwiGLU via Triton.

-(expert, m-tile, n-tile) via precomputed lookup tables, so there is no

+A single 2-D kernel launch handles every expert simultaneously.

+its (m_tile, n_tile) from pid1; blocks with tile_idx >= expert_num_tiles exit

Actually, before that, let me check if there might be an issue with the kernel itself causing the slowdown. Let me verify the timing with a simpler test. Wait, I just realized something. The benchmark.py calls `time_fn` which might include warm-up. Let me check what time_fn does. Actually, looking at the benchmark output: - eager: 25.2 ms - compiled: 25.3 ms - solution: 48.4 ms Our solution is 2x SLOWER than PyTorch eager! That's terrible. The eager reference loops over experts in Python and uses `torch.matmul` for each expert. Even though the problem says "the whole point is to implement the variable-length grouped GEMM yourself", using a poorly optimized custom kernel that's 2x slower than eager is not good. The PyTorch eager implementation does: - For each expert: `x_e @ W_gate[e]` and `x_e @ W_up[e]` - This uses cuBLAS, which is highly optimized - cuBLAS can achieve near-peak performance on each individual GEMM Our Triton kernel is much less optimized than cuBLAS. So the challenge is to beat or match cuBLAS performance with a custom kernel. For grouped GEMM, the key advantages of a custom kernel are: 1. Fusing the two GEMMs + SwiGLU into one kernel, reducing memory bandwidth 2. Better load balancing across experts 3. Reduced kernel launch overhead But our current kernel might not be well-tuned. Let me think about how to optimize it. Actually, let me reconsider the approach. Instead of a 2D grid with one block per tile, what if I use a 1D grid where each block handles multiple tiles? Or what if I use larger blocks? Another idea: **Use TMA (Tensor Memory Accelerator) on Blackwell**. TMA can dramatically improve memory bandwidth by using hardware-accelerated tensor loads. Triton 3.6 might support TMA. Actually, let me check if Triton 3.6 supports TMA on Blackwell.

Triton 3.6.0 has `tl.make_tensor_descriptor` which is the TMA API. TMA on Blackwell can significantly improve memory bandwidth and reduce register pressure. However, TMA is complex to use and might not be straightforward for a grouped GEMM. Let me first try simpler optimizations. Let me think about the performance gap. Our kernel is 2x slower than eager PyTorch+cuBLAS. The eager version: - Launches 128 separate matmuls per GEMM (gate and up) - Each matmul is a well-optimized cuBLAS kernel For a single expert with M=2048, K=4096, N=1536: - cuBLAS can easily achieve >100 TFLOPS for this size - Total time for 128 experts * 2 GEMMs = ~25ms Our single kernel achieves 17 TFLOPS overall. That's quite low. Let me try a different strategy: instead of a single kernel for all experts, what if I use a single kernel per expert but with better block sizes? Or what if I use a single kernel with a 1D grid that maps blocks to output tiles more directly? Actually, I wonder if the 2D grid is causing problems. CUDA grids with dimension > 65535 in any single dimension might have issues, but 192 is well within limits. Wait, I just realized: in our kernel, each block exits early if `tile_idx >= total_tiles`. For the balanced case, all experts have the same number of tiles, so no blocks exit early. But the grid is computed as `(E, num_m * num_n)` where `num_m = cdiv(max_n, BLOCK_M)`. For balanced routing with exact division, this is exact. Let me try different block sizes to see if performance improves. +"""Quick config sweep for the grouped GEMM kernel."""
+import sys
+from pathlib import Path
+sys.path.insert(0, str(Path(__file__).parent))
+
+import torch
+import triton
+import triton.language as tl
+import time
+
+from solution import _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel, Model, _build_routing
+
+# Use shape 1 (small) for fast iteration
+T_total, H, I, E, K = 4096, 2048, 1024, 64, 4
+
+device = torch.device("cuda:0")
+model = Model(T_total, H, I, E, K).to(device).eval()
+hidden_states = torch.randn(T_total * K, H, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
+expert_offsets = _build_routing(T_total, E, K, device=device)
+
+out = torch.empty(T_total * K, I, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device)
+
+off_cpu = expert_offsets.cpu()
+max_n = max(int(off_cpu[e+1].item()) - int(off_cpu[e].item()) for e in range(E))
+
+configs = [
+    (64, 64, 64, 4, 3),
+    (64, 128, 64, 4, 3),
+    (128, 64, 64, 4, 3),
+    (128, 128, 64, 4, 3),
+    (128, 128, 64, 8, 3),
+    (64, 256, 64, 8, 3),
+    (128, 256, 64, 8, 3),
+    (256, 64, 64, 8, 3),
+    (256, 128, 64, 8, 3),
+    (64, 64, 128, 4, 3),
+    (128, 128, 128, 8, 3),
+    (64, 128, 128, 8, 3),
+]
+
+# Warmup CUDA
+torch.cuda.synchronize()
+
+for bm, bn, bk, nw, ns in configs:
+    num_m = (max_n + bm - 1) // bm
+    num_n = (I + bn - 1) // bn
+    grid = (E, num_m * num_n)
+    
+    # Warmup
+    for _ in range(3):
+        _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel[grid](
+            hidden_states, model.W_gate, model.W_up, expert_offsets, out,
+            hidden_states.stride(0), hidden_states.stride(1),
+            model.W_gate.stride(0), model.W_gate.stride(1), model.W_gate.stride(2),
+            model.W_up.stride(0), model.W_up.stride(1), model.W_up.stride(2),
+            out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
+            H, I,
+            BLOCK_M=bm, BLOCK_N=bn, BLOCK_K=bk, num_warps=nw, num_stages=ns,
+        )
+    torch.cuda.synchronize()
+    
+    # Time
+    start = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
+    end = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
+    start.record()
+    for _ in range(10):
+        _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel[grid](
+            hidden_states, model.W_gate, model.W_up, expert_offsets, out,
+            hidden_states.stride(0), hidden_states.stride(1),
+            model.W_gate.stride(0), model.W_gate.stride(1), model.W_gate.stride(2),
+            model.W_up.stride(0), model.W_up.stride(1), model.W_up.stride(2),
+            out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
+            H, I,
+            BLOCK_M=bm, BLOCK_N=bn, BLOCK_K=bk, num_warps=nw, num_stages=ns,
+        )
+    end.record()
+    torch.cuda.synchronize()
+    ms = start.elapsed_time(end) / 10.0
+    
+    flops = 2 * T_total * H * (2 * I)
+    tflops = flops / (ms * 1e-3) / 1e12
+    print(f"BM={bm:3d} BN={bn:3d} BK={bk:3d} NW={nw} NS={ns} | {ms:.3f} ms | {tflops:.1f} TFLOPS")

content (2749 chars)
"""Quick config sweep for the grouped GEMM kernel."""
import sys
from pathlib import Path
sys.path.insert(0, str(Path(__file__).parent))

import torch
import triton
import triton.language as tl
import time

from solution import _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel, Model, _build_routing

# Use shape 1 (small) for fast iteration
T_total, H, I, E, K = 4096, 2048, 1024, 64, 4

device = torch.device("cuda:0")
model = Model(T_total, H, I, E, K).to(device).eval()
hidden_states = torch.randn(T_total * K, H, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
expert_offsets = _build_routing(T_total, E, K, device=device)

out = torch.empty(T_total * K, I, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device)

off_cpu = expert_offsets.cpu()
max_n = max(int(off_cpu[e+1].item()) - int(off_cpu[e].item()) for e in range(E))

configs = [
    (64, 64, 64, 4, 3),
    (64, 128, 64, 4, 3),
    (128, 64, 64, 4, 3),
    (128, 128, 64, 4, 3),
    (128, 128, 64, 8, 3),
    (64, 256, 64, 8, 3),
    (128, 256, 64, 8, 3),
    (256, 64, 64, 8, 3),
    (256, 128, 64, 8, 3),
    (64, 64, 128, 4, 3),
    (128, 128, 128, 8, 3),
    (64, 128, 128, 8, 3),
]

# Warmup CUDA
torch.cuda.synchronize()

for bm, bn, bk, nw, ns in configs:
    num_m = (max_n + bm - 1) // bm
    num_n = (I + bn - 1) // bn
    grid = (E, num_m * num_n)
    
    # Warmup
    for _ in range(3):
        _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel[grid](
            hidden_states, model.W_gate, model.W_up, expert_offsets, out,
            hidden_states.stride(0), hidden_states.stride(1),
            model.W_gate.stride(0), model.W_gate.stride(1), model.W_gate.stride(2),
            model.W_up.stride(0), model.W_up.stride(1), model.W_up.stride(2),
            out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
            H, I,
            BLOCK_M=bm, BLOCK_N=bn, BLOCK_K=bk, num_warps=nw, num_stages=ns,
        )
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    
    # Time
    start = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    end = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    start.record()
    for _ in range(10):
        _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel[grid](
            hidden_states, model.W_gate, model.W_up, expert_offsets, out,
            hidden_states.stride(0), hidden_states.stride(1),
            model.W_gate.stride(0), model.W_gate.stride(1), model.W_gate.stride(2),
            model.W_up.stride(0), model.W_up.stride(1), model.W_up.stride(2),
            out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
            H, I,
            BLOCK_M=bm, BLOCK_N=bn, BLOCK_K=bk, num_warps=nw, num_stages=ns,
        )
    end.record()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    ms = start.elapsed_time(end) / 10.0
    
    flops = 2 * T_total * H * (2 * I)
    tflops = flops / (ms * 1e-3) / 1e12
    print(f"BM={bm:3d} BN={bn:3d} BK={bk:3d} NW={nw} NS={ns} | {ms:.3f} ms | {tflops:.1f} TFLOPS")

Very interesting results! Key findings: 1. **BM=256, BN=64, BK=64, NW=8, NS=3 is the best**: 61.6 TFLOPS for the small shape 2. **128x128 with NW=4 is terrible** (1.1-1.3 TFLOPS) - probably register spilling or bad scheduling 3. **128x128 with NW=8 or 16 is decent** (~41 TFLOPS) 4. **256x128 is also terrible with NW=8** - same issue 5. **Larger BLOCK_M seems better** when BLOCK_N is small The pattern seems to be: - BLOCK_N should be 64 for best performance - BLOCK_M should be as large as possible (256 is best) - BLOCK_K=64 is fine - num_warps=8 works well - num_stages=3 is best when shared memory allows This makes sense: with BLOCK_N=64, we have less register pressure. With BLOCK_M=256, each block processes more rows, which amortizes the weight loading overhead. But wait, why is 128x128 with NW=4 so bad while 128x128 with NW=8 is fine? +"""Quick config sweep for the headline shape."""
+import sys
+from pathlib import Path
+sys.path.insert(0, str(Path(__file__).parent))
+sys.path.insert(0, str(Path(__file__).parent.parent))
+
+import torch
+
+from solution import _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel, Model, _build_routing
+
+# Headline shape
+T_total, H, I, E, K = 32768, 4096, 1536, 128, 8
+
+device = torch.device("cuda:0")
+model = Model(T_total, H, I, E, K).to(device).eval()
+hidden_states = torch.randn(T_total * K, H, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
+expert_offsets = _build_routing(T_total, E, K, device=device)
+
+out = torch.empty(T_total * K, I, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device)
+
+off_cpu = expert_offsets.cpu()
+max_n = max(int(off_cpu[e+1].item()) - int(off_cpu[e].item()) for e in range(E))
+
+configs = [
+    (128, 64, 64, 8, 3),
+    (256, 64, 64, 8, 3),
+    (256, 64, 64, 8, 2),
+    (128, 128, 64, 8, 3),
+    (128, 128, 64, 8, 2),
+    (64, 64, 64, 4, 3),
+    (64, 128, 64, 8, 3),
+]
+
+# Warmup CUDA
+torch.cuda.synchronize()
+
+for bm, bn, bk, nw, ns in configs:
+    num_m = (max_n + bm - 1) // bm
+    num_n = (I + bn - 1) // bn
+    grid = (E, num_m * num_n)
+    
+    try:
+        # Warmup
+        for _ in range(3):
+            _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel[grid](
+                hidden_states, model.W_gate, model.W_up, expert_offsets, out,
+                hidden_states.stride(0), hidden_states.stride(1),
+                model.W_gate.stride(0), model.W_gate.stride(1), model.W_gate.stride(2),
+                model.W_up.stride(0), model.W_up.stride(1), model.W_up.stride(2),
+                out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
+                H, I,
+                BLOCK_M=bm, BLOCK_N=bn, BLOCK_K=bk, num_warps=nw, num_stages=ns,
+            )
+        torch.cuda.synchronize()
+        
+        # Time
+        start = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
+        end = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
+        start.record()
+        for _ in range(10):
+            _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel[grid](
+                hidden_states, model.W_gate, model.W_up, expert_offsets, out,
+                hidden_states.stride(0), hidden_states.stride(1),
+                model.W_gate.stride(0), model.W_gate.stride(1), model.W_gate.stride(2),
+                model.W_up.stride(0), model.W_up.stride(1), model.W_up.stride(2),
+                out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
+                H, I,
+                BLOCK_M=bm, BLOCK_N=bn, BLOCK_K=bk, num_warps=nw, num_stages=ns,
+            )
+        end.record()
+        torch.cuda.synchronize()
+        ms = start.elapsed_time(end) / 10.0
+        
+        flops = 2 * T_total * H * (2 * I)
+        tflops = flops / (ms * 1e-3) / 1e12
+        print(f"BM={bm:3d} BN={bn:3d} BK={bk:3d} NW={nw:2d} NS={ns} | {ms:.3f} ms | {tflops:.1f} TFLOPS")
+    except Exception as e:
+        print(f"BM={bm:3d} BN={bn:3d} BK={bk:3d} NW={nw:2d} NS={ns} | ERROR: {e}")
