reasoning (24688 chars)
The headline shape performance is much worse than the small shape. Best is 20.3 TFLOPS with BM=256, BN=64, BK=64, NW=8, NS=2. That's still only 10% of peak. For the small shape, we got 61.6 TFLOPS (30% of peak). The difference is likely due to: 1. **H=4096 vs H=2048**: The K-loop is twice as long for the headline shape 2. **I=1536 vs I=1024**: More N tiles 3. **E=128 vs E=64**: More experts, but same total tokens per expert With H=4096, each block does 4096/64 = 64 iterations of the K-loop. In each iteration, it loads x (256*64*2 = 32KB), wg (64*64*2 = 8KB), wu (8KB). Total per iteration = 48KB. Over 64 iterations = 3MB per block. But with 128 experts and num_m=8, num_n=24, total blocks = 128*192 = 24576. Total memory moved = 24576 * 3MB = 73GB But the actual unique data is: - x: 262144 * 4096 * 2 = 2.1GB - wg + wu: 128 * 4096 * 1536 * 2 * 2 = 3.2GB - out: 262144 * 1536 * 2 = 0.8GB Total = 6.1GB So we're reading 73GB from memory but only 6.1GB is unique. This means each data element is read ~12 times on average. This is because: - Each x row is loaded by num_n = 24 blocks (one per n-tile) - Each weight element is loaded by num_m = 8 blocks (one per m-tile) Wait, actually for x: - Each row of x (for a given expert) is loaded by all n-tile blocks for that expert's m-tile. - For expert e with M_e=2048 and BLOCK_M=256, there are 8 m-tiles. - Each x row in m-tile i is loaded by 24 n-tile blocks. - So each x row is loaded 24 times. For weights: - Each weight block (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N) is loaded by all m-tile blocks. - So each weight element is loaded 8 times. This is a lot of redundant loading! The L2 cache might help, but with 128 experts and total weight size 3.2GB, the weights for different experts don't share cache. For the small shape: - M_e=256, BLOCK_M=256, num_m=1 - x is loaded only once per row (by all 16 n-tiles, but wait...) Actually, for the small shape with BM=256: - num_m = 1, num_n = 16 - Each x row is loaded 16 times (once per n-tile block) - Each weight element is loaded 1 time So x is loaded 16 times, weights 1 time. Total memory = 16 * 2.1GB + 1 * 3.2GB ≈ 37GB But our benchmark showed 61.6 TFLOPS. The actual time was 0.558ms for 67G FLOPs. Hmm, let me recalculate memory bandwidth for the small shape: - Time = 0.558ms - Total data moved ≈ 37GB (estimated with cache effects) - Bandwidth = 37GB / 0.558ms = 66 TB/s - impossible! Wait, my calculation is wrong. The unique data for small shape: - x: 16384 * 2048 * 2 = 64MB - wg + wu: 64 * 2048 * 1024 * 4 = 512MB - out: 16384 * 1024 * 2 = 32MB - Total = 608MB If x is loaded 16 times: 16 * 64MB = 1GB If weights are loaded 1 time: 512MB Output written 1 time: 32MB Total estimated = 1.5GB Bandwidth = 1.5GB / 0.558ms = 2.7 TB/s That's higher than the peak bandwidth of 1.8 TB/s, but cache effects and L2 hit rates mean it's not all going to DRAM. Some of the x reuse happens in L2 cache. For the headline shape with BM=256, BN=64: - num_m = 8, num_n = 24 - x loaded 24 times per row - weights loaded 8 times per element - Unique data: x=2.1GB, weights=3.2GB, out=0.8GB - Estimated total: 24*2.1 + 8*3.2 + 0.8 = 50.4 + 25.6 + 0.8 = 76.8GB - Time = 40.6ms - Bandwidth = 76.8GB / 40.6ms = 1.9 TB/s That's close to the peak bandwidth! So the headline shape might be memory-bandwidth limited. If we're memory-bandwidth limited, the only way to speed up is to reduce memory traffic. How? 1. **Fuse multiple n-tiles into one block**: If a block processes multiple N tiles sequentially, it can reuse the x load. - For example, a block could process BLOCK_M rows and all N columns, instead of just BLOCK_N columns. - But this increases register pressure because the accumulator is larger. 2. **Increase BLOCK_N**: Process more N columns per block, reducing the number of times x is loaded. - But we saw that BLOCK_N=128 with NW=8 causes issues for some configs. 3. **Use persistent kernels or cooperative groups**: Have blocks cooperate to load shared data. Actually, the real solution is to **increase BLOCK_N** so that each block processes more output columns, thereby reusing x more. But we need to do this without causing the terrible performance we saw with some configs. Wait, the terrible performance with 128x128 NW=4 might be a red herring. Let me test 128x128 more carefully. Actually, looking at the results again: - 128x128 NW=8 NS=3: 41.7 TFLOPS for small shape, 17.2 TFLOPS for headline - 256x64 NW=8 NS=2: 56.1 TFLOPS for small shape, 20.3 TFLOPS for headline For headline shape: - 128x128: num_m=16, num_n=12, x loaded 12 times, weights loaded 16 times - 256x64: num_m=8, num_n=24, x loaded 24 times, weights loaded 8 times Estimated traffic for 128x128: - 12*2.1 + 16*3.2 + 0.8 = 25.2 + 51.2 + 0.8 = 77.2GB - Time = 47.9ms - Bandwidth = 77.2 / 47.9 = 1.61 TB/s For 256x64: - 24*2.1 + 8*3.2 + 0.8 = 50.4 + 25.6 + 0.8 = 76.8GB - Time = 40.6ms - Bandwidth = 76.8 / 40.6 = 1.89 TB/s So 256x64 achieves higher effective bandwidth, which explains why it's faster. But can we do better? What if we use a config where x is loaded fewer times? For BLOCK_M=128, BLOCK_N=256: - num_m=16, num_n=6 - x loaded 6 times, weights loaded 16 times - Traffic = 6*2.1 + 16*3.2 + 0.8 = 12.6 + 51.2 + 0.8 = 64.6GB - If bandwidth = 1.8 TB/s, time = 64.6/1800 = 35.9ms But we saw that 64x256 crashes with OOM. Let me try 128x256 with fewer warps/stages. Actually, the issue with large BLOCK_N is shared memory. For BLOCK_N=256, BLOCK_K=64: - wg: 64*256*2 = 32KB, wu: 32KB - x: 128*64*2 = 16KB - Total per stage: 80KB - With num_stages=2 for pipelining: 160KB - might exceed limits Let me try num_stages=1 for larger block sizes. Actually, I realize there might be another issue. For headline shape with H=4096 and BLOCK_K=64, the K-loop has 64 iterations. With num_stages=3, Triton does software pipelining, which increases shared memory usage but also increases occupancy by overlapping memory and compute. Wait, let me reconsider. Maybe I should look at this from a different angle. What if I use a **1D grid** where each block handles a larger tile? Or what if I process multiple N tiles within the same block? For example, I could have each block compute all N columns for a given M tile. The block would: 1. Load x once 2. Loop over N in chunks of BLOCK_N 3. For each N chunk, load the corresponding weights and compute gate+up But this increases register pressure because we need accumulators for all N columns. With I=1536 and BLOCK_M=128, we'd need 128*1536*2*4 = 1.5MB of accumulators - impossible in registers. Alternatively, we could keep x in shared memory and reuse it across N tiles. But shared memory is also limited. Actually, a better idea: **Use the "concatenated weights" approach** like the DeepSeek-V4-Pro solution. Instead of loading W_gate and W_up separately, concatenate them into (E, H, 2*I). Then each block does ONE tl.dot with a (BLOCK_K, 2*BLOCK_N) weight matrix, and splits the result in the epilogue. This reduces the number of weight loads by half! Because instead of loading wg and wu separately, we load one combined weight matrix. Let me think about this: - W_combined = [W_gate | W_up] of shape (E, H, 2*I) - For a given x tile and n_start, we load W_combined[e, k:k+BLOCK_K, n_start:n_start+BLOCK_N] and W_combined[e, k:k+BLOCK_K, I+n_start:I+n_start+BLOCK_N] - Wait, these are not contiguous in memory. They're BLOCK_N apart. - So we'd still need two separate loads. Unless we load the full (BLOCK_K, 2*BLOCK_N) and then split it: - W_combined[e, k:k+BLOCK_K, n_start:n_start+2*BLOCK_N] where n_start is in [0, I) for the first call and [I, 2*I) for the second... no, that doesn't work because we'd be computing a (BLOCK_M, 2*BLOCK_N) output. Actually, if we compute a (BLOCK_M, 2*BLOCK_N) output: - gate = first BLOCK_N columns - up = second BLOCK_N columns - Then apply SwiGLU: silu(gate) * up - But we only want to store I columns, not 2*I So for each n_start in [0, I) with step BLOCK_N: - Load W_slice = W_combined[e, k:k+BLOCK_K, n_start:n_start+BLOCK_N] and W_combined[e, k:k+BLOCK_K, I+n_start:I+n_start+BLOCK_N] - These are two separate loads, BLOCK_N apart. Unless BLOCK_N = I (1536), we'd need to load two slices. If BLOCK_N=1536, we'd load the full width. But then shared memory would be: x(128*64*2=16KB) + W(64*1536*2*2=384KB) - way too much. So concatenating weights doesn't help with memory bandwidth for the weight loads. What about **transposing the weights** so that N is the leading dimension? If W is (E, I, H) instead of (E, H, I): - For loading (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N) where BLOCK_K corresponds to H and BLOCK_N corresponds to I: - W_t[e, n_start:n_start+BLOCK_N, k_start:k_start+BLOCK_K] - This is contiguous in K (H) dimension but strided in N (I) dimension. - For tl.dot, we want the K dimension to be contiguous in the second operand. - If W_t is (I, H) with stride H in I and 1 in H, then: - W_t[n, k] has stride H in n, 1 in k - For tile (n_start:n_start+BLOCK_N, k_start:k_start+BLOCK_K): - ptrs = base + offs_n[:, None] * H + offs_k[None, :] * 1 - This has shape (BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K), not (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N) - We'd need to transpose it for tl.dot: `tl.dot(x, w.T)` Does `tl.dot` with a transpose work efficiently in Triton? I think it does, but it might not use tensor cores optimally. Actually, let me try a different approach: **increase BLOCK_M to reduce weight loads**. With BLOCK_M=256, each weight block is loaded by 8 m-tiles (for headline shape). With BLOCK_M=512, each weight block is loaded by 4 m-tiles. With BLOCK_M=1024, each weight block is loaded by 2 m-tiles. With BLOCK_M=2048, each weight block is loaded by 1 m-tile. But larger BLOCK_M means more shared memory for x and more register pressure. Let me try BLOCK_M=512, BLOCK_N=64, BLOCK_K=64. Also, let me try BLOCK_K=128 to reduce the number of K-loop iterations and potentially improve pipelining. Wait, I just realized: for the headline shape with BLOCK_M=256, BLOCK_N=64, the grid is (128, 192) = 24576 blocks. Each SM on Blackwell can handle multiple blocks. With 144 SMs and 24576 blocks, that's ~170 blocks per SM. Each block with NW=8 has 256 threads. At max occupancy of 2048 threads per SM, we can fit 8 blocks per SM concurrently. So total concurrent blocks = 144 * 8 = 1152. Total waves = 24576 / 1152 ≈ 21 waves. That's a lot of waves! Each wave takes some time. If a block takes ~1.5ms to complete (just a guess), then 21 waves might take ~30ms. Our actual time is 40ms, which is in the ballpark. To reduce time, we need either: 1. Fewer blocks (larger tiles) 2. Faster blocks (better utilization) Let me try BLOCK_M=512 or even 1024. But these might cause register pressure issues. Also, what about BLOCK_N=128 with a working config? We saw that 128x128 NW=8 NS=3 gives 41.7 TFLOPS for small shape but only 17.2 for headline. Let me try 256x128 with NW=16 or other configs to see if we can make it work. Actually, looking at the terrible configs (128x128 NW=4, 256x128 NW=8), they all have a common pattern: BLOCK_M * BLOCK_N is large (16384 or 32768) and num_warps is "wrong". This might be a Triton bug. Let me systematically test larger BLOCK_N with different num_warps. But wait, I should also think about another optimization: **process the gate and up GEMMs with a single tl.dot on concatenated weights, but load the concatenated block as a single contiguous chunk**. If W_combined is (E, H, 2*I) and we want to load both gate and up weights for a given (k, n_start): - The gate weights are at W_combined[e, k:k+BLOCK_K, n_start:n_start+BLOCK_N] - The up weights are at W_combined[e, k:k+BLOCK_K, I+n_start:I+n_start+BLOCK_N] - The distance between them is I - BLOCK_N columns. - If I is large (1536) and BLOCK_N is small (64), they're far apart. But what if we load them as a single chunk of shape (BLOCK_K, 2*BLOCK_N) from W_combined[e, k:k+BLOCK_K, n_start:n_start+2*BLOCK_N]? That only works if n_start + 2*BLOCK_N <= I, which is not true for most n_start. Alternatively, what if we restructure the weights so that gate and up are interleaved? Like (E, H, I, 2) where the last dimension is [gate, up]. Then for a given (k, n_start), gate and up are adjacent: - W_interleaved[e, k, n_start, :] has shape (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N, 2) - We can load this as one chunk and split. But PyTorch stores (E, H, I, 2) with the last dimension contiguous. So W[e, k, n, 0] and W[e, k, n, 1] are adjacent. For a tile (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N), the elements are at: - W[e, k:k+BLOCK_K, n_start:n_start+BLOCK_N, 0:2] - This is a 3D tensor of shape (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N, 2) For tl.dot, we'd need to reshape this. `tl.dot` expects 2D matrices. We could load as (BLOCK_K, 2*BLOCK_N) by flattening the last two dimensions. But then the gate and up results would be interleaved in the output, and we'd need to separate them in the epilogue. Actually, this might work! Let me think: - W_flat = W_interleaved.reshape(E, H, 2*I) -- but this doesn't change the memory layout - Actually, if W_interleaved is (E, H, I, 2), then W_interleaved[e, k, n, 0] = W_gate[e, k, n], W_interleaved[e, k, n, 1] = W_up[e, k, n] - If we view this as (E, H, 2*I), then W_flat[e, k, 2*n] = W_gate[e, k, n] and W_flat[e, k, 2*n+1] = W_up[e, k, n] For a given n_start, we want: - Gate weights: W_flat[e, k, 2*n_start:2*(n_start+BLOCK_N):2] -- strided by 2 - Up weights: W_flat[e, k, 2*n_start+1:2*(n_start+BLOCK_N)+1:2] -- strided by 2 This is not contiguous and would be inefficient to load. What if we transpose differently? W of shape (E, H, 2, I) with layout: - W[e, k, 0, n] = W_gate[e, k, n] - W[e, k, 1, n] = W_up[e, k, n] Then for a tile: - Gate: W[e, k:k+BLOCK_K, 0, n_start:n_start+BLOCK_N] - Up: W[e, k:k+BLOCK_K, 1, n_start:n_start+BLOCK_N] - The gate and up are separated by I*BLOCK_K elements in memory. Still not contiguous. What about W of shape (2, E, H, I)? Then gate is W[0] and up is W[1]. Same issue. The fundamental problem is that gate and up weights are separate tensors, and loading both requires two memory transactions. We can't easily fuse them without restructuring the data layout. OK, let me try another angle: **What if I use a different kernel design?** Instead of a 2D grid where each block handles one tile, what if I use a 1D grid and each block handles ALL N columns for a given M slice? This is like having each block compute a full row-tile output. For example, with BLOCK_M=64 and I=1536: - Each block computes 64 rows and all 1536 columns - But 64*1536*2*4 bytes of accumulators = 768KB - way too much for registers What if each block computes a sub-range of N columns, but loops over multiple M tiles? No, that doesn't reduce memory traffic. Another idea: **Persist x in shared memory across N tiles**. In the current kernel, for a given expert and m_tile, each n_tile block loads the same x slice independently. If we could have blocks cooperate to load x once and share it, we'd reduce x memory traffic by num_n times. But CUDA/Triton blocks don't share memory directly. However, if multiple blocks run on the same SM sequentially, x might stay in L1 cache. But we can't rely on this. A persistent kernel approach: - Launch a fixed number of blocks (e.g., num_SMs * occupancy) - Each block processes multiple tiles from a work queue - A block loads x into shared memory, then processes all N tiles for that M slice before moving to the next M slice But implementing a work queue in Triton is complex. We'd need atomic counters and shared memory for the queue. Actually, there's a simpler variant: **have each block process multiple N tiles sequentially**. In the current kernel, a block with (expert_id, tile_idx) processes one (m_tile, n_tile). What if we change it so that: - pid0 = expert_id - pid1 = m_tile - The block loops over all n_tiles for that m_tile This way, x is loaded once into shared memory (or registers), and then reused for all n_tiles. Let's calculate the resource usage for this: - BLOCK_M=64, BLOCK_N=64, I=1536 - num_n_tiles = 24 - x: 64*64*2 = 8KB (if BLOCK_K=64) - wg: 64*64*2 = 8KB - wu: 64*64*2 = 8KB - Acc_gate: 64*64*4 = 16KB (registers) - Acc_up: 64*64*4 = 16KB (registers) But the block needs to loop over 24 n_tiles. In each iteration, it loads different wg/wu but reuses x. The K-loop still runs 64 times per n_tile. Wait, if we keep x in shared memory, we don't need to reload it for each n_tile. We load x once (for the current K block), then for each n_tile, load wg/wu and compute dot. But the dot product accumulates over K. So we'd need to: 1. For k = 0 to H step BLOCK_K: a. Load x_k into shared memory b. For each n_tile: i. Load wg_k_n and wu_k_n ii. acc_gate += dot(x_k, wg_k_n) iii. acc_up += dot(x_k, wu_k_n) This means we need separate accumulators for each n_tile. With 24 n_tiles and BLOCK_M=64, BLOCK_N=64: - acc_gate total size: 24 * 64 * 64 * 4 = 393KB - This is way too much for registers. Unless we process n_tiles one at a time but keep x in shared memory. Then accumulators are just 64*64*4 = 16KB per n_tile, but we need to reload x for each K block... wait, no, if we keep x in shared memory across the K-loop, we can reuse it. Actually, we can do: 1. For k = 0 to H step BLOCK_K: a. Load x_k into shared memory b. For n_tile = 0 to num_n_tiles: i. Load wg_k_n and wu_k_n ii. acc_gate = dot(x_k, wg_k_n) iii. acc_up = dot(x_k, wu_k_n) iv. After K-loop completes, apply SwiGLU and store Wait, no, the dot accumulates over K. So we need to keep acc_gate and acc_up across K iterations. We could keep them in shared memory or registers. If we process n_tiles sequentially with BLOCK_N=64: - acc_gate: 64*64*4 = 16KB (can fit in registers for NW=4 or 8) - x_k in shared memory: 64*64*2 = 8KB - wg_k_n in shared memory: 64*64*2 = 8KB - wu_k_n in shared memory: 64*64*2 = 8KB - Total shared memory: 24KB This seems feasible! Each block: - Expert_id from pid0, m_tile from pid1 - Loop over n_tiles (from 0 to num_n_tiles-1) - For each n_tile, loop over K - But x is reloaded only once per K block, not once per n_tile Actually, we still need to reload x for each K block. The savings is: for a fixed K block and M slice, x is loaded once and reused across all N tiles. In the original kernel, x was loaded num_n_tiles times per K block. So memory traffic for x is reduced by num_n_tiles. For headline shape with BLOCK_M=64, BLOCK_N=64, I=1536: - num_n_tiles = 24 - x traffic reduced by 24x - New traffic: x = 2.1GB/24 = 0.088GB, weights = 3.2GB * (2048/64) = wait... Actually, for weights: each weight block is loaded once per (m_tile, n_tile). With the new approach, each block processes all n_tiles for one m_tile. So weight traffic per m_tile is num_n_times * BLOCK_K * BLOCK_N * 2 * 2. For the full expert: - Original: num_m * num_n blocks, each loading its own wg/wu - New: num_m blocks, each loading num_n wg/wu slices - Total weight traffic is the same But x traffic is reduced by num_n. For headline shape: - New estimated traffic: x = 2.1GB/24 = 0.088GB, weights = 3.2GB * (num_m for BLOCK_M=64 is 32) = wait... Let me think again. In the original kernel with BLOCK_M=256, BN=64: - num_m = 8, num_n = 24 - Each x row loaded 24 times - Each weight element loaded 8 times - Total: 24*2.1 + 8*3.2 + 0.8 = 76GB In the new kernel with BLOCK_M=64, BN=64, each block handles all 24 n_tiles: - num_m = 32 (blocks per expert) - Each x row loaded once (per block, but each row is in exactly one block) - Each weight element loaded 32 times (once per m_tile block) - Total: 1*2.1 + 32*3.2 + 0.8 = 104.9GB Hmm, that's worse! Because with smaller BLOCK_M, weights are loaded more times. What if we use BLOCK_M=256, BN=64, and each block handles all 24 n_tiles? - num_m = 8 - Each x row loaded once - Each weight element loaded 8 times - Total: 1*2.1 + 8*3.2 + 0.8 = 27.7GB That's much better! And with bandwidth of 1.8 TB/s, time = 27.7/1800 = 15.4ms. But wait, can we fit the accumulators for 24 n_tiles in registers? - With BLOCK_M=256, BLOCK_N=64: - Acc per n_tile: 256*64*4 = 64KB (fp32) - For 24 n_tiles: 1.5MB - This is way too much for registers. What if we process n_tiles in groups? Say, 4 n_tiles at a time: - Acc: 4 * 64KB = 256KB - Still a lot, but maybe manageable with NW=16 (512 threads) - 256KB / 512 = 512 bytes/thread = 128 fp32 regs - Plus other registers. Might be tight but possible. Or we can keep accumulators in shared memory and stream through n_tiles. Actually, let me think differently. What if we keep BLOCK_M=256, BLOCK_N=64, and process n_tiles sequentially, but store partial accumulators in shared memory? Shared memory is 128KB per SM. For one block: - x: 256*64*2 = 32KB - wg: 64*64*2 = 8KB - wu: 64*64*2 = 8KB - acc_gate (for current n_tile): 256*64*4 = 64KB - acc_up (for current n_tile): 64KB - Total = 176KB - exceeds shared memory. So we can't store both accumulators in shared memory. What if we process gate and up sequentially? First compute gate for all n_tiles, then compute up for all n_tiles? That doubles the x loads. Hmm, this is getting complicated. Let me step back. Actually, the most important thing is to try the config that reduces x traffic. Let me try a simpler variant: **Have each block handle one expert and process all (m, n) tiles sequentially.** Grid = (E, 1). Each block loops over all m_tiles and n_tiles for its expert. - x is loaded once per (m_tile, k) and kept in shared memory - wg and wu are loaded per (m_tile, n_tile, k) - Accumulators are in registers, one (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N) per loop With BLOCK_M=256, BLOCK_N=64, I=1536, M_e=2048: - num_m = 8, num_n = 24 - Each block processes 8*24 = 192 tiles serially - Total blocks = 128 - Each block has 8 warps = 256 threads Memory traffic: - x: loaded 8 times per expert (once per m_tile, reused across 24 n_tiles) - Wait, x is reloaded for each K block. For H=4096, BK=64, there are 64 K blocks. - Per m_tile, x is loaded 64 times. - Per expert, x is loaded 8*64 = 512 times. - Each x load is 256*64*2 = 32KB. - Total x traffic per expert = 512 * 32KB = 16MB. - For 128 experts = 2GB. - wg/wu: loaded 8*24*64 = 12288 times per expert - Each load is 64*64*2 = 8KB. - Total per expert = 12288 * 8KB = 96MB. - For 128 experts = 12.3GB. Wait, I think my analysis is getting confused. Let me be more careful. In the sequential approach, for each (m_tile, n_tile): - Loop over K: load x_k, wg_k_n, wu_k_n, accumulate - x_k is loaded for each K block and each n_tile - So x is NOT reused across n_tiles unless we explicitly keep it in shared memory If we keep x_k in shared memory across n_tiles: - For k=0: - Load x_0 into shared memory - For n_tile=0 to 23: - Load wg_0_n, wu_0_n - acc_gate += dot(x_0, wg_0_n) - acc_up += dot(x_0, wu_0_n) - Load x_1 into shared memory - For n_tile=0 to 23: - Load wg_1_n, wu_1_n - acc_gate += dot(x_1, wg_1_n) - acc_up += dot(x_1, wu_1_n) But acc_gate and acc_up need to be kept across K iterations. If we process all n_tiles for k=0, then all n_tiles for k=1, etc., we'd need 24 separate accumulators (one per n_tile). With BLOCK_M=64, BLOCK_N=64: - 24 accumulators * 64*64*4 = 393KB - too much for registers or shared memory. What if we process n_tiles in batches? Batch size = 4: - 4 accumulators * 64*64*4 = 64KB - manageable in registers with enough warps - x_k in shared memory: 64*64*2 = 8KB - wg_k_n in shared memory: 4 * 64*64*2 = 32KB - wu_k_n in shared memory: 32KB - Total shared: 72KB - OK But then we need 6 batches (24/4) per K block. +"""More config sweep for the headline shape."""
+import sys
+from pathlib import Path
+sys.path.insert(0, str(Path(__file__).parent))
+sys.path.insert(0, str(Path(__file__).parent.parent))
+
+import torch
+
+from solution import _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel, Model, _build_routing
+
+# Headline shape
+T_total, H, I, E, K = 32768, 4096, 1536, 128, 8
+
+device = torch.device("cuda:0")
+model = Model(T_total, H, I, E, K).to(device).eval()
+hidden_states = torch.randn(T_total * K, H, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
+expert_offsets = _build_routing(T_total, E, K, device=device)
+
+out = torch.empty(T_total * K, I, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device)
+
+off_cpu = expert_offsets.cpu()
+max_n = max(int(off_cpu[e+1].item()) - int(off_cpu[e].item()) for e in range(E))
+
+configs = [
+    # (bm, bn, bk, nw, ns)
+    (256, 64, 64, 8, 1),
+    (256, 64, 64, 8, 2),
+    (256, 64, 64, 8, 3),
+    (256, 64, 64, 16, 1),
+    (256, 64, 64, 16, 2),
+    (512, 64, 64, 8, 1),
+    (512, 64, 64, 8, 2),
+    (512, 64, 64, 16, 1),
+    (512, 64, 64, 16, 2),
+    (128, 64, 128, 8, 1),
+    (128, 64, 128, 8, 2),
+    (128, 64, 128, 16, 1),
+    (256, 64, 128, 8, 1),
+    (256, 64, 128, 16, 1),
+    (128, 128, 64, 8, 1),
+    (128, 128, 64, 8, 2),
+    (128, 128, 64, 16, 1),
+    (256, 128, 64, 16, 1),
+    (64, 64, 64, 8, 3),
+    (64, 128, 64, 8, 3),
+    (64, 256, 64, 8, 1),
+    (128, 256, 64, 8, 1),
+    (128, 256, 64, 16, 1),
+]
+
+# Warmup CUDA
+torch.cuda.synchronize()
+
+for bm, bn, bk, nw, ns in configs:
+    num_m = (max_n + bm - 1) // bm
+    num_n = (I + bn - 1) // bn
+    grid = (E, num_m * num_n)
+    
+    try:
+        # Warmup
+        for _ in range(3):
+            _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel[grid](
+                hidden_states, model.W_gate, model.W_up, expert_offsets, out,
+                hidden_states.stride(0), hidden_states.stride(1),
+                model.W_gate.stride(0), model.W_gate.stride(1), model.W_gate.stride(2),
+                model.W_up.stride(0), model.W_up.stride(1), model.W_up.stride(2),
+                out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
+                H, I,
+                BLOCK_M=bm, BLOCK_N=bn, BLOCK_K=bk, num_warps=nw, num_stages=ns,
+            )
+        torch.cuda.synchronize()
+        
+        # Time
+        start = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
+        end = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
+        start.record()
+        for _ in range(10):
+            _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel[grid](
+                hidden_states, model.W_gate, model.W_up, expert_offsets, out,
+                hidden_states.stride(0), hidden_states.stride(1),
+                model.W_gate.stride(0), model.W_gate.stride(1), model.W_gate.stride(2),
+                model.W_up.stride(0), model.W_up.stride(1), model.W_up.stride(2),
+                out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
+                H, I,
+                BLOCK_M=bm, BLOCK_N=bn, BLOCK_K=bk, num_warps=nw, num_stages=ns,
+            )
+        end.record()
+        torch.cuda.synchronize()
+        ms = start.elapsed_time(end) / 10.0
+        
+        flops = 2 * T_total * H * (2 * I)
+        tflops = flops / (ms * 1e-3) / 1e12
+        print(f"BM={bm:3d} BN={bn:3d} BK={bk:3d} NW={nw:2d} NS={ns} | {ms:.3f} ms | {tflops:.1f} TFLOPS")
+    except Exception as e:
+        print(f"BM={bm:3d} BN={bn:3d} BK={bk:3d} NW={nw:2d} NS={ns} | ERROR: {e}")

For a given m_tile and k_block, ALL n_tiles load the SAME x slice. This is a huge amount of redundant memory traffic. For headline shape with BM=256, BN=64: - num_n = 24 - Each x slice (256 rows, 64 cols of K) is loaded 24 times - If we could share x across n_tiles, we'd reduce x traffic by 24x But as I analyzed before, sharing x across n_tiles requires either: 1. Larger BLOCK_N (but we saw performance issues with large BLOCK_N) 2. Persistent kernel with work queue (complex) 3. Sequential processing of n_tiles within a block (requires keeping accumulators) Actually, wait. I just realized there might be a much simpler approach: **What if we transpose the computation?** Instead of computing gate = x @ W_gate and up = x @ W_up, what if we compute: - gate_t = W_gate_t @ x_t - up_t = W_up_t @ x_t Where x_t is (H, T_perm), W_gate_t is (I, H), gate_t is (I, T_perm). In standard GEMM terms: - Original: M=T_perm, K=H, N=I, C = A @ B - Transposed: M=I, K=H, N=T_perm, C = B_t @ A_t But this doesn't change the fundamental memory access pattern. Wait, actually it does! In the original: - x is accessed row-major (M, K) - W is accessed row-major (K, N) - Output is row-major (M, N) In the transposed view: - W_t is (N, K) row-major - x_t is (K, M) row-major - Output_t is (N, M) row-major For a block computing (BLOCK_N, BLOCK_M) of output_t: - Load W_t slice: (BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K) from (N, K) - K contiguous - Load x_t slice: (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_M) from (K, M) - M contiguous - Compute dot The memory access pattern is essentially the same, just with dimensions swapped. Hmm, but what if we transpose the weights at init time? W of shape (E, H, I) -> W_t of shape (E, I, H). Then for expert e, W_t[e] is (I, H). For a block computing output tile (m_start, n_start): - x slice: (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K) from (T_perm, H) - W slice: (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N) from (H, I) -- this is the original If W_t is (I, H): - W_t slice: (BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K) from (I, H) - For dot: we need (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N), so we'd use W_t.T Actually, loading W_t as (BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K) and transposing in shared memory might have better coalescing if BLOCK_N > BLOCK_K. CUTLASS Python DSL is available. Let me explore what it offers. But I should also consider the complexity. The user said "your call" on the approach. Given that: 1. Triton is working but performance is only ~20 TFLOPS on headline shape (10% of peak) 2. CUTLASS might offer better performance but is more complex 3. The deadline is "take as long as you need" but I should be efficient Let me first understand why the Triton kernel is slow. Let me profile it with ncu to see the actual bottleneck. Actually, before investing time in CUTLASS, let me try one more Triton optimization: **using a single weight tensor with concatenated gate+up**. If we store W_combined as (E, H, 2*I), and have each block load a (BLOCK_K, 2*BLOCK_N) slice, then we only need ONE tl.dot call per K iteration instead of TWO. This reduces the number of dot instructions and might improve instruction throughput. Wait, we'd still need two separate accumulators for gate and up. But we could compute: ```python W_combined_slice = tl.load(...) # (BLOCK_K, 2*BLOCK_N) # Split into gate and up weights # But W_combined is [gate | up], so columns [0, BLOCK_N) are gate, [BLOCK_N, 2*BLOCK_N) are up # We can load this as one (BLOCK_K, 2*BLOCK_N) tensor acc = tl.dot(x, W_combined_slice) # (BLOCK_M, 2*BLOCK_N) # Then split acc into gate and up gate = acc[:, 0:BLOCK_N] up = acc[:, BLOCK_N:2*BLOCK_N] ``` This replaces TWO tl.dot calls with ONE tl.dot call! Since each dot call is expensive (tensor core instruction), this could improve performance significantly. But wait, W_combined[e, k:k+BLOCK_K, n_start:n_start+2*BLOCK_N] is only contiguous if n_start + 2*BLOCK_N <= I. For n_start near I - BLOCK_N, we'd need to load across the gate/up boundary, which isn't contiguous. Actually, we could restructure W_combined differently. +"""Test 256x128 and other configs with different warp counts."""
+import sys

+

+
+T_total, H, I, E, K = 32768, 4096, 1536, 128, 8
+
+device = torch.device("cuda:0")
+model = Model(T_total, H, I, E, K).to(device).eval()
+hidden_states = torch.randn(T_total * K, H, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
+expert_offsets = _build_routing(T_total, E, K, device=device)
+
+out = torch.empty(T_total * K, I, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device)
+
+off_cpu = expert_offsets.cpu()
+max_n = max(int(off_cpu[e+1].item()) - int(off_cpu[e].item()) for e in range(E))
+
+configs = [
+    (256, 128, 64, 4, 1),
+    (256, 128, 64, 4, 2),
+    (256, 128, 64, 8, 1),
+    (256, 128, 64, 8, 2),
+    (256, 128, 64, 8, 3),
+    (128, 128, 64, 4, 1),
+    (128, 128, 64, 4, 2),
+    (128, 128, 64, 4, 3),
+    (256, 64, 64, 4, 1),
+    (256, 64, 64, 4, 2),
+    (256, 64, 64, 4, 3),
+]
+
+# Warmup CUDA
+torch.cuda.synchronize()
+
+for bm, bn, bk, nw, ns in configs:
+    num_m = (max_n + bm - 1) // bm
+    num_n = (I + bn - 1) // bn
+    grid = (E, num_m * num_n)
+    
+    try:
+        for _ in range(3):
+            _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel[grid](
+                hidden_states, model.W_gate, model.W_up, expert_offsets, out,
+                hidden_states.stride(0), hidden_states.stride(1),
+                model.W_gate.stride(0), model.W_gate.stride(1), model.W_gate.stride(2),
+                model.W_up.stride(0), model.W_up.stride(1), model.W_up.stride(2),
+                out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
+                H, I,
+                BLOCK_M=bm, BLOCK_N=bn, BLOCK_K=bk, num_warps=nw, num_stages=ns,
+            )
+        torch.cuda.synchronize()
+        
+        start = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
+        end = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
+        start.record()
+        for _ in range(10):
+            _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel[grid](
+                hidden_states, model.W_gate, model.W_up, expert_offsets, out,
+                hidden_states.stride(0), hidden_states.stride(1),
+                model.W_gate.stride(0), model.W_gate.stride(1), model.W_gate.stride(2),
+                model.W_up.stride(0), model.W_up.stride(1), model.W_up.stride(2),
+                out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
+                H, I,
+                BLOCK_M=bm, BLOCK_N=bn, BLOCK_K=bk, num_warps=nw, num_stages=ns,
+            )
+        end.record()
+        torch.cuda.synchronize()
+        ms = start.elapsed_time(end) / 10.0
+        
+        flops = 2 * T_total * H * (2 * I)
+        tflops = flops / (ms * 1e-3) / 1e12
+        print(f"BM={bm:3d} BN={bn:3d} BK={bk:3d} NW={nw:2d} NS={ns} | {ms:.3f} ms | {tflops:.1f} TFLOPS")
+    except Exception as e:
+        print(f"BM={bm:3d} BN={bn:3d} BK={bk:3d} NW={nw:2d} NS={ns} | ERROR: {e}")

We copy expert_offsets to CPU every forward pass! And then we compute max_n on CPU. This `expert_offsets.cpu()` call causes a device-to-host synchronization and memcpy. This is a HUGE bottleneck! For the headline shape, `expert_offsets` is only 129 int32 elements (516 bytes). But the `.cpu()` call synchronizes the CUDA stream, causing a massive stall. Let me fix this by computing max_n on GPU or caching the result. Actually, `expert_offsets` is passed as a tensor. It might already be on GPU (since check.py moves inputs to device). So `.cpu()` copies from GPU to CPU, which requires stream synchronization. For a benchmark that calls forward many times, each call syncs the stream. That's terrible! The fix is simple: compute max_n on GPU without copying to CPU. ```python max_n = int((expert_offsets[1:] - expert_offsets[:-1]).max().item()) ``` But `.item()` also causes a device-to-host sync! We need to avoid `.item()` entirely. In PyTorch, if we want to compute grid size from tensor values, we have a few options: 1. Use `torch.compile` to capture the scalar value in the graph 2. Precompute on CPU when the tensor is created 3. Use a fixed large grid and let blocks exit early Option 3 is the simplest! Instead of computing `max_n` and using it for grid sizing, we can use a fixed grid size that's large enough for any possible expert_offsets. For example: ```python # Maximum possible tokens per expert = T_perm grid = (E, ((T_perm + BLOCK_M - 1) // BLOCK_M) * ((I + BLOCK_N - 1) // BLOCK_N)) ``` But this would launch many more blocks than needed, and most would exit early. For balanced routing, all experts have the same M_e, so no waste. But for arbitrary offsets, some experts might have fewer tokens. Wait, we can compute a tighter bound. The maximum tokens per expert is at most T_perm (if all tokens go to one expert). So: ```python max_possible_m = T_perm ``` But that's very loose for balanced routing. Let me think of a better approach. Actually, we can compute `max_n` once during `forward` and keep it as a Python scalar. But `forward` is called many times. What if we cache the grid? We can compare `expert_offsets` with a cached version. ```python if not hasattr(self, '_cached_offsets') or not torch.equal(expert_offsets, self._cached_offsets): self._cached_offsets = expert_offsets.clone() self._cached_max_n = int((expert_offsets[1:] - expert_offsets[:-1]).max().item()) max_n = self._cached_max_n ``` But `.item()` still causes a sync the first time. However, for the benchmark, `time_fn` does warm-up iterations, so the sync happens during warm-up and the timed iterations use the cached value. Actually, wait. `time_fn` might create new inputs for each iteration, or it might reuse the same inputs. Let me check. reasoning (3901 chars)
So `time_fn` does 10 warm-up iterations and 30 timed iterations. Each timed iteration is preceded by an L2 flush and synchronization. If our forward method calls `.cpu()` or `.item()` on a GPU tensor, it will cause a device-to-host synchronization on EVERY forward call. This is a huge bottleneck. Let me fix the forward method to avoid any CPU synchronization. Option 1: Cache the grid metadata based on the tensor's storage pointer. Option 2: Compute everything on GPU. Option 3: Use a fixed maximum grid size. For option 3, the simplest fix is: ```python # Compute max_n on GPU, but avoid .item() by using a precomputed upper bound # For any valid expert_offsets, max_n <= T_perm T_perm = hidden_states.shape[0] max_n = T_perm # Upper bound ``` Then the grid is: ```python num_m = (max_n + BLOCK_M - 1) // BLOCK_M num_n = (I + BLOCK_N - 1) // BLOCK_N grid = (E, num_m * num_n) ``` But this would launch E * num_m * num_n blocks, where num_m = cdiv(T_perm, BLOCK_M). For headline shape: T_perm = 262144, BLOCK_M = 256, num_m = 1024. Grid = (128, 1024 * 24) = (128, 24576) = 3,145,728 blocks! Most of these blocks would exit early because each expert only has 2048 tokens (num_m_tiles = 8). So we'd launch 1024/8 = 128x more blocks than needed. This would cause massive overhead from block scheduling and early exits. Not good. Option 2: Compute max_n on GPU and pass it to the kernel. We can compute `max_n` as a GPU scalar and pass it to the kernel. But the grid size needs to be a Python integer, not a GPU tensor. Wait, what if we launch the kernel with a fixed grid and let the kernel compute its own assignment? For example: - Grid = (E, some_large_number) where the second dimension is an upper bound - In the kernel, each block checks if its m_tile and n_tile are valid But we still need to know `num_m_tiles` per expert, which varies. Actually, there's a clever trick: **launch with grid=(E, 1) and have each block loop over all tiles**. This is the "one block per expert" approach. For expert e: - M_e = expert_end - expert_begin - num_m = cdiv(M_e, BLOCK_M) - num_n = cdiv(I, BLOCK_N) - The block loops over all m_tiles and n_tiles This avoids needing to know max_n for grid sizing. The grid is just (E, 1) or (E,). But as I analyzed before, this serializes a lot of work in each block. With 188 SMs and only 128 blocks, we'd only use 128 SMs. And each block does a lot of work. Wait, but what if we launch more blocks per expert? For example, grid=(E, num_parallel_blocks) where each block handles a subset of tiles. Actually, let me think about this differently. What if we precompute `max_n` using a cached hash but avoid the `.item()` sync by doing it asynchronously? No, `.item()` is inherently synchronous. What if we compute `max_n` on CPU when the Model is initialized? The `_build_routing` function is deterministic for a given shape. But `expert_offsets` is passed at forward time and might vary. Actually, looking at check.py and benchmark.py, `expert_offsets` comes from `reference.get_inputs()` which calls `_build_routing`. For a given shape, `_build_routing` always produces the same offsets. So we could precompute the grid at init time. But to be general, we should handle arbitrary expert_offsets. Here's a good compromise: 1. Use a cache keyed by `expert_offsets.data_ptr()` or a hash of the tensor 2. The first time we see a new tensor, we compute `max_n` on CPU (with `.cpu().max().item()`) 3. +"""Test if .cpu() sync is the bottleneck."""
+import sys
+from pathlib import Path
+sys.path.insert(0, str(Path(__file__).parent.parent))
+
+import torch
+import time
+
+from solution import Model, _build_routing
+
+T_total, H, I, E, K = 32768, 4096, 1536, 128, 8
+device = torch.device("cuda:0")
+model = Model(T_total, H, I, E, K).to(device).eval()
+x = torch.randn(T_total * K, H, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
+offsets = _build_routing(T_total, E, K, device=device)
+
+# Warmup
+for _ in range(3):
+    _ = model(x, offsets)
+torch.cuda.synchronize()
+
+start = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
+end = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
+start.record()
+for _ in range(10):
+    _ = model(x, offsets)
+end.record()
+torch.cuda.synchronize()
+print(f"With .cpu(): {start.elapsed_time(end) / 10:.3f} ms")
+
+# Now test without .cpu() by monkey-patching
+class FastModel(Model):
+    def forward(self, hidden_states, expert_offsets):
+        T_perm, H_val = hidden_states.shape
+        I_val = self.I
+        E_val = self.E
+        out = torch.empty(T_perm, I_val, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=hidden_states.device)
+        
+        # Avoid .cpu() - use a cached/precomputed max_n
+        max_n = T_perm  # loose upper bound
+        
+        BLOCK_M = 256
+        BLOCK_N = 64
+        BLOCK_K = 64
+        num_m = (max_n + BLOCK_M - 1) // BLOCK_M
+        num_n = (I_val + BLOCK_N - 1) // BLOCK_N
+        grid = (E_val, num_m * num_n)
+        
+        from solution import _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel
+        _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel[grid](
+            hidden_states, self.W_gate, self.W_up, expert_offsets, out,
+            hidden_states.stride(0), hidden_states.stride(1),
+            self.W_gate.stride(0), self.W_gate.stride(1), self.W_gate.stride(2),
+            self.W_up.stride(0), self.W_up.stride(1), self.W_up.stride(2),
+            out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
+            H_val, I_val,
+            BLOCK_M=BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N=BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K=BLOCK_K,
+            num_warps=8, num_stages=1,
+        )
+        return out
+
+fast_model = FastModel(T_total, H, I, E, K).to(device).eval()
+fast_model.load_state_dict(model.state_dict())
+
+for _ in range(3):
+    _ = fast_model(x, offsets)
+torch.cuda.synchronize()
+
+start = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
+end = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
+start.record()
+for _ in range(10):
+    _ = fast_model(x, offsets)
+end.record()
+torch.cuda.synchronize()
+print(f"Without .cpu() (T_perm bound): {start.elapsed_time(end) / 10:.3f} ms")

reasoning (1588 chars)
The `.cpu()` sync adds about 6.5ms (48.4ms vs 41.9ms). That's significant but not the main bottleneck. The kernel itself is still taking ~42ms. So the performance issue is mainly the kernel, not the CPU sync. Let me think about what else we can optimize. With the T_perm bound grid (which launches way too many blocks), we still get 41.9ms. That's only slightly worse than the optimized grid (38-40ms). So grid sizing is not the main issue. The main issue is that our kernel is slow compared to cuBLAS. cuBLAS does the same work in ~25ms. One key difference: cuBLAS processes each expert's GEMM as a separate kernel, which means: 1. L2 cache is fully utilized for that expert's weights 2. No cross-expert cache pollution 3. Tensor core pipelines are optimally filled Our single kernel processes all experts, and blocks from different experts might interleave, causing L2 cache thrashing. Wait, actually, in CUDA block scheduling, blocks are typically scheduled in order. For a 2D grid (128, 192), blocks are scheduled in some order. If they're scheduled as (0,0), (0,1), ..., (0,191), (1,0), ..., then all blocks for expert 0 run first. This should be good for cache locality. But if they're scheduled in some other order (e.g., round-robin across experts), then cache locality would be poor. Actually, for a 2D grid, the exact scheduling order depends on the hardware and driver. But typically, blocks with nearby IDs run on the same SM. For a 2D grid of (128, 192), blocks might be scheduled with some locality. Let me try the per-expert launch approach and see if it's faster.

--- /dev/null
+++ b/scratch/test_per_expert.py
@@ -0,0 +1,157 @@
+"""Test per-expert kernel launch vs single kernel."""
+import sys
+from pathlib import Path
+sys.path.insert(0, str(Path(__file__).parent.parent))
+
+import torch
+import triton
+import triton.language as tl
+
+from solution import Model, _build_routing
+
+T_total, H, I, E, K = 32768, 4096, 1536, 128, 8
+device = torch.device("cuda:0")
+model = Model(T_total, H, I, E, K).to(device).eval()
+x = torch.randn(T_total * K, H, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
+offsets = _build_routing(T_total, E, K, device=device)
+
+@triton.jit
+def _single_expert_kernel(
+    x_ptr, w_gate_ptr, w_up_ptr, out_ptr,
+    stride_x_m, stride_x_k,
+    stride_wg_k, stride_wg_n,
+    stride_wu_k, stride_wu_n,
+    stride_o_m, stride_o_n,
+    M, N, K,
+    BLOCK_M: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr,
+):
+    pid = tl.program_id(0)
+    num_pid_m = tl.cdiv(M, BLOCK_M)
+    num_pid_n = tl.cdiv(N, BLOCK_N)
+    pid_m = pid // num_pid_n
+    pid_n = pid % num_pid_n
+    
+    m_start = pid_m * BLOCK_M
+    n_start = pid_n * BLOCK_N
+    
+    x_base = x_ptr + m_start * stride_x_m
+    wg_base = w_gate_ptr + n_start * stride_wg_n
+    wu_base = w_up_ptr + n_start * stride_wu_n
+    o_base = out_ptr + m_start * stride_o_m + n_start * stride_o_n
+    
+    acc_gate = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.float32)
+    acc_up = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.float32)
+    
+    offs_m = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_M)
+    offs_n = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)
+    offs_k = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K)
+    
+    m_mask = (m_start + offs_m)[:, None] < M
+    n_mask = (n_start + offs_n)[None, :] < N
+    
+    for k in range(0, K, BLOCK_K):
+        k_mask = (k + offs_k)[None, :] < K
+        x = tl.load(x_base + offs_m[:, None] * stride_x_m + (k + offs_k)[None, :] * stride_x_k,
+                    mask=m_mask & k_mask, other=0.0)
+        wg = tl.load(wg_base + (k + offs_k)[:, None] * stride_wg_k + offs_n[None, :] * stride_wg_n,
+                     mask=k_mask.T & n_mask, other=0.0)
+        wu = tl.load(wu_base + (k + offs_k)[:, None] * stride_wu_k + offs_n[None, :] * stride_wu_n,
+                     mask=k_mask.T & n_mask, other=0.0)
+        acc_gate = tl.dot(x, wg, acc_gate)
+        acc_up = tl.dot(x, wu, acc_up)
+    
+    silu_gate = acc_gate * tl.sigmoid(acc_gate)
+    out_val = (silu_gate * acc_up).to(tl.bfloat16)
+    tl.store(o_base + offs_m[:, None] * stride_o_m + offs_n[None, :] * stride_o_n,
+             out_val, mask=m_mask & n_mask)
+
+out = torch.empty(T_total * K, I, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device)
+off_cpu = offsets.cpu()
+
+BLOCK_M = 256
+BLOCK_N = 64
+BLOCK_K = 64
+
+# Warmup
+for _ in range(3):
+    for e in range(E):
+        start = int(off_cpu[e].item())
+        end = int(off_cpu[e+1].item())
+        M_e = end - start
+        if M_e == 0:
+            continue
+        grid = ((M_e + BLOCK_M - 1) // BLOCK_M * (I + BLOCK_N - 1) // BLOCK_N,)
+        _single_expert_kernel[grid](
+            x[start:end], model.W_gate[e], model.W_up[e], out[start:end],
+            x.stride(0), x.stride(1),
+            model.W_gate.stride(1), model.W_gate.stride(2),
+            model.W_up.stride(1), model.W_up.stride(2),
+            out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
+            M_e, I, H,
+            BLOCK_M=BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N=BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K=BLOCK_K,
+            num_warps=8, num_stages=1,
+        )
+torch.cuda.synchronize()
+
+start = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
+end = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
+start.record()
+for _ in range(10):
+    for e in range(E):
+        start_idx = int(off_cpu[e].item())
+        end_idx = int(off_cpu[e+1].item())
+        M_e = end_idx - start_idx
+        if M_e == 0:
+            continue
+        grid = ((M_e + BLOCK_M - 1) // BLOCK_M * (I + BLOCK_N - 1) // BLOCK_N,)
+        _single_expert_kernel[grid](
+            x[start_idx:end_idx], model.W_gate[e], model.W_up[e], out[start_idx:end_idx],
+            x.stride(0), x.stride(1),
+            model.W_gate.stride(1), model.W_gate.stride(2),
+            model.W_up.stride(1), model.W_up.stride(2),
+            out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
+            M_e, I, H,
+            BLOCK_M=BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N=BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K=BLOCK_K,
+            num_warps=8, num_stages=1,
+        )
+end.record()
+torch.cuda.synchronize()
+print(f"Per-expert launch: {start.elapsed_time(end) / 10:.3f} ms")
+
+# Compare with single kernel
+from solution import _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel
+max_n = max(int(off_cpu[e+1].item()) - int(off_cpu[e].item()) for e in range(E))
+num_m = (max_n + BLOCK_M - 1) // BLOCK_M
+num_n = (I + BLOCK_N - 1) // BLOCK_N
+grid = (E, num_m * num_n)
+
+for _ in range(3):
+    _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel[grid](
+        x, model.W_gate, model.W_up, offsets, out,
+        x.stride(0), x.stride(1),
+        model.W_gate.stride(0), model.W_gate.stride(1), model.W_gate.stride(2),
+        model.W_up.stride(0), model.W_up.stride(1), model.W_up.stride(2),
+        out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
+        H, I,
+        BLOCK_M=BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N=BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K=BLOCK_K,
+        num_warps=8, num_stages=1,
+    )
+torch.cuda.synchronize()
+
+start = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
+end = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
+start.record()
+for _ in range(10):

In our kernel: ```python wg_ptrs = wg_base + (k + offs_k)[:, None] * stride_wg_k + offs_n[None, :] * stride_wg_n ``` With `stride_wg_k = I = 1536` and `stride_wg_n = 1`. For a warp of 32 threads, how are the threads mapped to the load? In Triton, for a 2D tensor of shape (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N), threads are typically mapped to contiguous elements in the last dimension. So for `wg` of shape (64, 64), a warp might handle 32 contiguous elements along BLOCK_N (columns 0-31) for a specific row. Since `stride_wg_n = 1`, these 32 elements are contiguous in memory. This is coalesced. For the next row, the elements are at `ptr + I`. Since I=1536, these are 1536 elements apart. The warp loads 32 contiguous elements for each row. This should be fully coalesced. Each warp does 64 rows * 32 cols = 2048 elements, organized as 64 memory transactions of 32 contiguous elements. Wait, but in Triton, `tl.load` of a (64, 64) tensor with one warp might not use 64 separate transactions. Triton vectorizes loads. For bf16, it might load 16 elements at a time (32 bytes). So each row requires 4 vectorized loads (64/16=4). Total transactions = 64*4 = 256 per warp. This seems fine. What about x? ```python x_ptrs = x_base + offs_m[:, None] * stride_x_m + (k + offs_k)[None, :] * stride_x_k ``` With `stride_x_m = H = 4096` and `stride_x_k = 1`. x has shape (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_K) = (256, 64). Threads are mapped to contiguous elements along BLOCK_K. For a warp, it loads 32 contiguous elements along K for a specific row. Then 256 rows / 8 warps = 32 rows per warp. Wait, with 8 warps and 256 threads, how are threads mapped? In Triton, threads are typically organized with the last dimension contiguous. So thread 0 handles element (0, 0), thread 1 handles (0, 1), ..., thread 63 handles (0, 63), thread 64 handles (1, 0), etc. Actually, with BLOCK_K=64 and 256 threads, each thread handles one element in K and 4 elements in M (256 threads / 64 K = 4 M per thread). Or maybe 256 threads = 4 warps, and each warp handles 64/4 = 16 K elements? The exact thread mapping is determined by Triton's compiler. But for our purposes, what matters is that elements along K are contiguous in memory (stride=1), so the load should be coalesced. Let me try a different approach: **use a smaller BLOCK_K to see if it changes performance**. Wait, we already tested BK=32 and BK=64. Let me test BK=32 with larger blocks to see if shared memory allows more stages. Actually, another idea: **increase BLOCK_N to 128 but use num_stages=1 to avoid shared memory issues**. What about **BLOCK_M=256, BLOCK_N=32**? - x: 256*64*2 = 32KB - wg: 64*32*2 = 4KB - wu: 4KB - Total: 40KB - With NS=3: 120KB - might be tight Let me try a few more configs. Actually, I just realized something important. In our kernel, we do: ```python acc_gate = tl.dot(x, wg, acc_gate) acc_up = tl.dot(x, wu, acc_up) ``` These are two back-to-back dot operations using the same `x`. On some architectures, this is good because `x` stays in registers/shared memory. But on Blackwell, there might be a pipeline bubble between the two dot operations. What if we interleave the loads for the NEXT iteration with the dots of the CURRENT iteration? This is what software pipelining (num_stages > 1) does. But we're already using num_stages=1 for the best config. Wait, actually Triton might not be doing software pipelining for num_stages=1. With num_stages=2 or 3, it double/triple buffers the loads. But we saw that NS=2 and NS=3 are worse for the headline shape. Why would NS=2/3 be worse? With larger shared memory usage, fewer blocks can fit on an SM simultaneously. This reduces occupancy and might hurt performance more than the pipelining helps. For BLOCK_M=256, BLOCK_N=64, BLOCK_K=64: - x: 256*64*2 = 32KB - wg: 64*64*2 = 8KB - wu: 8KB - Total per stage: 48KB - With NS=2: 96KB - With NS=3: 144KB - exceeds 101KB limit! Wait, does NS=3 actually exceed the limit? Our kernel launched successfully with NS=3, but maybe Triton silently reduced the number of stages or spilled to registers. Actually, looking at the headline2 results: - 256x64 NW=8 NS=3: 47.1ms (worse than NS=1 and NS=2) - 256x64 NW=8 NS=2: 40.7ms - 256x64 NW=8 NS=1: 38.7ms So NS=1 is best. The pipelining overhead or reduced occupancy from NS>1 hurts performance. Let me think about other optimizations. One thing that might help is **using block pointers or descriptor-based loads** for better cache behavior. Another idea: **what if we transpose the weights at initialization so that the N dimension is contiguous in the K-loop?** Wait, the N dimension is already contiguous in our weight layout (stride_wg_n = 1). The K dimension has stride I. Actually, for tensor cores, the B matrix in C = A @ B should ideally have the K dimension as the leading dimension (i.e., B is column-major). Our W is row-major (K, N), which means the K dimension has stride N. In cuBLAS, weights are often stored in a special layout (e.g., TN format) that's optimized for tensor cores. Our row-major layout might cause extra transpose operations inside the tensor core. Does Triton handle this automatically? I think Triton tries to optimize the layout, but it might not be as good as cuBLAS's hand-tuned layouts. What if we explicitly transpose the weights to (N, K) and use `tl.dot(x, wg.T)`? +"""Test transposed weights."""
+import sys
+from pathlib import Path
+sys.path.insert(0, str(Path(__file__).parent.parent))
+
+import torch
+import triton
+import triton.language as tl
+
+from solution import Model, _build_routing
+
+T_total, H, I, E, K = 32768, 4096, 1536, 128, 8
+device = torch.device("cuda:0")
+model = Model(T_total, H, I, E, K).to(device).eval()
+x = torch.randn(T_total * K, H, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
+offsets = _build_routing(T_total, E, K, device=device)
+
+# Transpose weights: (E, H, I) -> (E, I, H)
+W_gate_t = model.W_gate.transpose(1, 2).contiguous()
+W_up_t = model.W_up.transpose(1, 2).contiguous()
+
+@triton.jit
+def _kernel_transposed(
+    x_ptr, w_gate_ptr, w_up_ptr, expert_offsets_ptr, out_ptr,
+    stride_x_m, stride_x_k,
+    stride_wg_e, stride_wg_n, stride_wg_k,
+    stride_wu_e, stride_wu_n, stride_wu_k,
+    stride_o_m, stride_o_n,
+    H, I,
+    BLOCK_M: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr,
+):
+    expert_id = tl.program_id(0)
+    tile_idx = tl.program_id(1)
+    
+    expert_begin = tl.load(expert_offsets_ptr + expert_id)
+    expert_end = tl.load(expert_offsets_ptr + expert_id + 1)
+    n_tokens = expert_end - expert_begin
+    if n_tokens <= 0:
+        return
+    
+    num_m_tiles = tl.cdiv(n_tokens, BLOCK_M)
+    num_n_tiles = tl.cdiv(I, BLOCK_N)
+    total_tiles = num_m_tiles * num_n_tiles
+    if tile_idx >= total_tiles:
+        return
+    
+    m_tile = tile_idx // num_n_tiles
+    n_tile = tile_idx % num_n_tiles
+    m_start = expert_begin + m_tile * BLOCK_M
+    n_start = n_tile * BLOCK_N
+    
+    x_base = x_ptr + m_start * stride_x_m
+    wg_base = w_gate_ptr + expert_id * stride_wg_e + n_start * stride_wg_n
+    wu_base = w_up_ptr + expert_id * stride_wu_e + n_start * stride_wu_n
+    o_base = out_ptr + m_start * stride_o_m + n_start * stride_o_n
+    
+    acc_gate = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.float32)
+    acc_up = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.float32)
+    
+    offs_m = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_M)
+    offs_n = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)
+    offs_k = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K)
+    
+    m_mask = (m_start + offs_m)[:, None] < expert_end
+    n_mask = (n_start + offs_n)[None, :] < I
+    
+    for k in range(0, H, BLOCK_K):
+        k_mask = (k + offs_k)[None, :] < H
+        x = tl.load(x_base + offs_m[:, None] * stride_x_m + (k + offs_k)[None, :] * stride_x_k,
+                    mask=m_mask & k_mask, other=0.0)
+        # wg is (I, H), load (BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K), transpose to (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N)
+        wg = tl.load(wg_base + (k + offs_k)[None, :] * stride_wg_k + offs_n[:, None] * stride_wg_n,
+                     mask=k_mask & n_mask.T, other=0.0)
+        wu = tl.load(wu_base + (k + offs_k)[None, :] * stride_wu_k + offs_n[:, None] * stride_wu_n,
+                     mask=k_mask & n_mask.T, other=0.0)
+        acc_gate = tl.dot(x, wg.T, acc_gate)
+        acc_up = tl.dot(x, wu.T, acc_up)
+    
+    silu_gate = acc_gate * tl.sigmoid(acc_gate)
+    out_val = (silu_gate * acc_up).to(tl.bfloat16)
+    tl.store(o_base + offs_m[:, None] * stride_o_m + offs_n[None, :] * stride_o_n,
+             out_val, mask=m_mask & n_mask)
+
+out = torch.empty(T_total * K, I, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device)
+off_cpu = offsets.cpu()
+max_n = max(int(off_cpu[e+1].item()) - int(off_cpu[e].item()) for e in range(E))
+
+BLOCK_M = 256
+BLOCK_N = 64
+BLOCK_K = 64
+num_m = (max_n + BLOCK_M - 1) // BLOCK_M
+num_n = (I + BLOCK_N - 1) // BLOCK_N
+grid = (E, num_m * num_n)
+
+for _ in range(3):
+    _kernel_transposed[grid](
+        x, W_gate_t, W_up_t, offsets, out,
+        x.stride(0), x.stride(1),
+        W_gate_t.stride(0), W_gate_t.stride(1), W_gate_t.stride(2),
+        W_up_t.stride(0), W_up_t.stride(1), W_up_t.stride(2),
+        out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
+        H, I,
+        BLOCK_M=BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N=BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K=BLOCK_K,
+        num_warps=8, num_stages=1,
+    )
+torch.cuda.synchronize()
+
+start = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
+end = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
+start.record()
+for _ in range(10):
+    _kernel_transposed[grid](
+        x, W_gate_t, W_up_t, offsets, out,
+        x.stride(0), x.stride(1),
+        W_gate_t.stride(0), W_gate_t.stride(1), W_gate_t.stride(2),
+        W_up_t.stride(0), W_up_t.stride(1), W_up_t.stride(2),
+        out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
+        H, I,
+        BLOCK_M=BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N=BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K=BLOCK_K,
+        num_warps=8, num_stages=1,
+    )
+end.record()
+torch.cuda.synchronize()
+ms = start.elapsed_time(end) / 10
+flops = 2 * T_total * H * (2 * I)
+tflops = flops / (ms * 1e-3) / 1e12

reasoning (2166 chars)
The SASS shows `MMA: 128` instructions but no `WGMMA` or `HGMMA`. This means Triton is using the old MMA instruction format for SM120. On Hopper (SM90), WGMMA is the preferred instruction. On Blackwell (SM100/SM120), there are newer instructions like `tcgen05.mma_async` or `HGMMA`. The fact that Triton is using `MMA` (which is the Ampere-style instruction) suggests Triton might not have full SM120 support, or it's falling back to a compatibility mode. But more importantly: **n_regs: 255, n_spills: 40**. This is the immediate performance killer. With 40 spilled registers, the kernel is doing a lot of local memory accesses, which are very slow. Why are there so many register spills? With BLOCK_M=256, BLOCK_N=64, BLOCK_K=64, num_warps=8 (256 threads): - Accumulators: 256*64*4*2 = 128KB for gate + up - At 255 registers per thread * 4 bytes * 256 threads = 260KB total registers - 128KB of accumulators leaves ~132KB for other variables - Plus the compiler needs registers for loop counters, pointers, masks, etc. - 40 spills means the compiler couldn't fit everything The spills are likely happening because the compiler is trying to keep too many values live simultaneously. How can we reduce register pressure? 1. **Smaller BLOCK_M or BLOCK_N**: This directly reduces accumulator size 2. **Fewer num_warps**: Fewer threads means more registers per thread, but fewer concurrent warps 3. **num_stages=1**: Reduces pipelining state We already use num_stages=1. Let me try reducing BLOCK_M to 128 or BLOCK_N to 32. But we saw that 128x64 was only 14.2 TFLOPS for headline shape. That's because it launches more blocks but each block is less efficient. Actually, the 14.2 TFLOPS might have been with NS=3. Let me retest 128x64 with NS=1. Wait, looking at headline3 results, I didn't test 128x64 with NW=8 NS=1 for headline. Let me test it. Also, let me try BLOCK_M=256, BLOCK_N=32, BLOCK_K=64. - Accumulators: 256*32*4*2 = 64KB - This is half the accumulator size, which should reduce register pressure significantly. And BLOCK_M=128, BLOCK_N=32, BLOCK_K=64: - Accumulators: 128*32*4*2 = 32KB - Even less register pressure Let me test these configs.

Actually, a 2D grid of (128, 192) is likely scheduled with the Y dimension varying fastest (depending on the hardware). On most NVIDIA GPUs, blocks are scheduled with X varying fastest within a cluster. So blocks (0,0), (0,1), (0,2), ... run near each other. If blocks (0,0), (0,1), (0,2) all share the same expert and m_tile but different n_tile, they all load the same x slice. Running them near each other means the x slice might still be in L1 cache. +for shape_idx, (T_total, H, I, E, K) in enumerate(shapes):
+    print(f"\n=== Shape {shape_idx}: T_total={T_total}, H={H}, I={I}, E={E}, K={K} ===")
+    model = Model(T_total, H, I, E, K).to(device).eval()
+    x = torch.randn(T_total * K, H, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
+    offsets = _build_routing(T_total, E, K, device=device)
+    out = torch.empty(T_total * K, I, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device)
+    
+    off_cpu = offsets.cpu()
+    max_n = max(int(off_cpu[e+1].item()) - int(off_cpu[e].item()) for e in range(E))
+    
+    for bm, bn, bk, nw, ns in configs:
+        num_m = (max_n + bm - 1) // bm
+        num_n = (I + bn - 1) // bn
+        grid = (E, num_m * num_n)
+        
+        try:
+            for _ in range(3):
+                _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel[grid](
+                    x, model.W_gate, model.W_up, offsets, out,
+                    x.stride(0), x.stride(1),
+                    model.W_gate.stride(0), model.W_gate.stride(1), model.W_gate.stride(2),
+                    model.W_up.stride(0), model.W_up.stride(1), model.W_up.stride(2),
+                    out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
+                    H, I,
+                    BLOCK_M=bm, BLOCK_N=bn, BLOCK_K=bk, num_warps=nw, num_stages=ns,
+                )
+            torch.cuda.synchronize()
+            
+            start = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
+            end = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
+            start.record()
+            for _ in range(10):
+                _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel[grid](
+                    x, model.W_gate, model.W_up, offsets, out,
+                    x.stride(0), x.stride(1),
+                    model.W_gate.stride(0), model.W_gate.stride(1), model.W_gate.stride(2),
+                    model.W_up.stride(0), model.W_up.stride(1), model.W_up.stride(2),
+                    out.stride(0), out.stride(1),
+                    H, I,
+                    BLOCK_M=bm, BLOCK_N=bn, BLOCK_K=bk, num_warps=nw, num_stages=ns,
+                )
+            end.record()
+            torch.cuda.synchronize()
+            ms = start.elapsed_time(end) / 10
+            flops = 2 * T_total * H * (2 * I)
+            tflops = flops / (ms * 1e-3) / 1e12
+            print(f"  BM={bm} BN={bn} BK={bk} NW={nw} NS={ns}: {ms:.3f} ms | {tflops:.1f} TFLOPS")
+        except Exception as e:
+            print(f"  BM={bm} BN={bn} BK={bk} NW={nw} NS={ns}: ERROR: {e}")

Great results! NW=16 with NS=2 is consistently the best across all shapes: - Shape 0: 21.6 TFLOPS (vs 21.3 for NW=8 NS=1) - Shape 1: 58.4 TFLOPS (vs 52.4 for NW=8 NS=1) - Shape 2: 31.8 TFLOPS (vs 27.0 for NW=8 NS=1) For shape 1, 58.4 TFLOPS is excellent - that's 29% of peak. For shape 0, 21.6 TFLOPS is still only 10.8% of peak, but it's an improvement. But wait, the benchmark.py uses `time_fn` with L2 flush, which might give different results than my raw timing. Let me update solution.py to use (256, 64, 64, 16, 2) and run benchmark.py. Also, I should fix the `.cpu()` sync issue in forward. Even though it's only ~6ms, we should eliminate it. For fixing the `.cpu()` issue, I can: 1. Cache the grid based on the shape parameters 2. Or precompute `max_n` at init time Actually, since `expert_offsets` comes from `_build_routing` which is deterministic for a given shape, and the shapes are fixed per Model instance (T_total, E, K are init params), I can precompute `max_n = T_total * K` as a loose upper bound. Wait, `T_total * K` is T_perm, which is the maximum possible tokens for any expert. Using this would make the grid = (E, cdiv(T_perm, BLOCK_M) * cdiv(I, BLOCK_N)), which is huge. For shape 0: grid = (128, 1024 * 24) = (128, 24576) = 3,145,728 blocks. Most would exit early. That would be terrible. Better approach: precompute `max_n` at init time based on the balanced routing assumption: ```python self._max_n_per_expert = (T_total * K + E - 1) // E ``` This is exact for balanced routing and a tight upper bound. Then in forward: ```python max_n = self._max_n_per_expert ``` No CPU sync needed! But what if `expert_offsets` is not balanced? The problem says it could be arbitrary. But check.py and benchmark.py always use balanced routing. To be safe, I can compute max_n on GPU once and cache it: ```python if not hasattr(self, '_cached_max_n') or self._cached_max_n is None: # Compute on GPU but avoid sync by using precomputed upper bound for grid # Actually, we need the value for grid sizing... pass ``` Hmm, the problem is that grid sizing requires a Python integer. We can't compute it on GPU without syncing. But we can use a cached value and check if expert_offsets changed: ```python if self._cached_offsets is None or not torch.equal(expert_offsets, self._cached_offsets): self._cached_offsets = expert_offsets.clone() self._cached_max_n = int((expert_offsets[1:] - expert_offsets[:-1]).max().item()) max_n = self._cached_max_n ``` The first call has a sync, but subsequent calls don't. For `time_fn`, the warm-up iterations will trigger the sync, and the timed iterations will use the cached value. Actually, looking at our test earlier, the `.cpu()` added only 6.5ms for 10 iterations (0.65ms per call). For benchmark.py's `time_fn` with 30 iterations, that's ~19.5ms extra. But with caching, this only happens once during warm-up. Let me implement caching and update the block config to (256, 64, 64, 16, 2). Actually, wait. `time_fn` does 10 warm-up iterations followed by 30 timed iterations. If caching kicks in during the first warm-up iteration, then all 9 remaining warm-up iterations and all 30 timed iterations are fast. Let me verify this by running benchmark.py after the fix. Also, I should consider whether there are any other configs that might be even better. Let me do a broader search with NW=16. Actually, let me first update solution.py and run benchmark.py.

         nn.init.normal_(self.W_gate, std=0.02)
         nn.init.normal_(self.W_up, std=0.02)
 
+        # Cache for grid metadata to avoid CPU sync in forward
+        self._cached_offsets: torch.Tensor | None = None
+        self._cached_max_n: int | None = None
+
+    def _get_max_n(self, expert_offsets: torch.Tensor) -> int:
+        """Return max tokens per expert, cached to avoid repeated D2H syncs."""
+        if self._cached_offsets is not None:
+            # Fast path: same tensor object (works when inputs are reused)
+            if expert_offsets.data_ptr() == self._cached_offsets.data_ptr():
+                return self._cached_max_n
+            # Slightly slower: same contents but different tensor object
+            if expert_offsets.shape == self._cached_offsets.shape:
+                if torch.equal(expert_offsets, self._cached_offsets):
+                    return self._cached_max_n
+        # Cache miss: compute on CPU (one-time cost per unique offsets)
+        self._cached_offsets = expert_offsets.clone()
+        self._cached_max_n = int((expert_offsets[1:] - expert_offsets[:-1]).max().item())
+        return self._cached_max_n
+
